And then care for the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the scab from the burning is gone.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and the wound are sealed.
And it is necessary to regulate their nourishment according to the length of their healing time, which will also affect their stomach, and to prevent the intestines from being forced out during the process of recovery.
And then, when approximately 40 days have passed, administer a properly made bandage, and let them wear it for another 40 days.
And let him/her be protected from work, overeating, and strong noises, for if he/she follows this regimen of treatment, complete healing will occur.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and if, at the beginning, you want to prevent them from coming back, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and that which has been excised from the lining should be the same size as one-third of it.  Then treat it as previously described, and it will not recur.
And some earlier practitioners would say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
And a line is placed along the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and then your hand is placed with a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first, lighter cautery is better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of twisting (or torsion).
When, in some limbs, pain arises from injury or impact, and that pain persists for a long time and you do not find a cure with medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be of every limb according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, the location of the twisting, and the pain in it.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, good.  And if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is the custom of those pains to be punctured on the limb closest to the affected one, so that you must treat these with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some leprous people receive great benefit from cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, if you wish to cauterize it, consider if the leprosy is superficial and has been treated by what we have said in the division, and is not spreading, and you fear that the corruption will encompass the entire body and constitution of the patient, then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: with one in the middle of the head, with a second below it, covering the forehead to the ends of the hair; and two on the temples; and one at the back, on the nape of the neck or its vicinity.
And finally, press the cautery slightly into the bone to create a small indentation, so that the expulsion of thick vapors may be eased from it; and cauterize the spleen according to what is necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is clearly evident, then you must cauterize it with those cauteries that we have mentioned for the head; and with one cautery on the tip of the nose, and two on each cheekbone, and two on each vertebra of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the chest, one on the chest, and two on the hips, one on each hip, and two on the groin, and one on each groin, and two on the thighs, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of each foot; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the armpits of the arms.
And be careful of the nerves that are near the heel, so you don't burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the pelvis, and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, just as they do not from needles, because the patient's body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as has been previously described by the types of instruments.
And then treat the cauterization with flour of herbs cooked with honey and the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the depth of the wound a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness is cauterized, which affects the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back, and the nerves that affect that limb, and the numbness is removed.
But do not presume upon this unless one is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the passage of the nerves throughout the entire moving body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of a skin disease called morphea.
When the skin disease is longstanding and you do not find any benefit from the usual medical ointments, then cauterize the affected area with a cautery, applying it for a brief time according to the extent of the lesion, until the whiteness and its color are gone, and the color of the area is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils, mixed with rose oil and arnica leaves, and with a part of dove's blood or swallow's blood, all combined.  Then apply a clean cloth over the area and leave it until healed.
Chapter 50.
Of cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around the affected area, as it is twisted around.
And the learned say it should be cauterized with a deeper cautery in the middle; but I do not see this, for I fear it will become ulcerated, and I have seen this happen several times.
And it is more correct to cauterize it around the affected area with a circular cautery, as we said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Of cauterization of swellings.
When a swelling has been delayed in maturation, and this is due to excess matter produced, or the patient's age (being old and frail, with little blood), or due to time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the swelling; then cauterize around it with many small, pointed cauteries; and afterward leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery—of which this is the shape—and place it in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a lower location, so that the arm's condition is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive (or ulcerative) condition.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption within the limb, which corrodes it as if by fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if this corrosive is in a place suitable for cauterization by fire, then use small, pointed cauteries, and larger ones depending on the size of the area affected by the corrosive.
And then cauterize it from every side until all the corrosion is eradicated and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply dressings soaked in oil mixed with [other ingredients] to the cauterized areas until all the scabs are removed and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-healing ointment.
But if after three days the healed flesh in the area appears healthy and free of corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes corrosive medicine is used as a substitute for cauterization. But cauterization with fire is a more readily effective treatment.
And I have already described its treatment with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore use it when needed.
Chapter 53.
Of cauterization of ulcers and other similar conditions.
Often in the parts beneath the sick foot there is an illness called an ulcer, a large, deep wound that troubles the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is done by igniting a hollow cautery, similar in shape to a small bird of prey, made of iron to the exact size required for the entire ulcer, and with fine edges/points.
Then place that ignited cautery over the ulcer, and then rotate your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it there for three days until it begins to form a scab. Then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for that will uproot it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you must apply cautery with water.  Take a cannula made of bronze, iron, or a bird's bone, and place it over the ulcer.  Pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, then hold your hand steady, rotating the cannula with pressure from your hand, making sure the cannula's edge presses against the base of the ulcer.  The hot water will travel to the root of the ulcer.  Allow the patient to endure the hot water's action for an hour, then remove it.  This will uproot the ulcer from its roots.
And thus do this to each one, and then to the others, until all such afflictions on their bodies are gone.
And then treat those pus wounds where the roots of the ulcers have been eradicated with ointments that promote flesh regeneration.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of tremor.
When someone has a tremor caused by cold in the nerves, or by a cold-causing fever, or by another similar cause, then you should cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on the soles of their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive oil cautery.
And the tremor subsides, hastening the health of the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the cauterization of white spots/accidents on the body.
When white spots and pustules appear on the body, which are caused by thick corrupted matter, then, as soon as it appears, you should cauterize the head of the fistula with a thin cautery made of myrtle wood, the end of which is heated; or with the roots of long-stemmed herbs; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes a cautery is also used at the beginning of its expulsion in this way; and it is not believed, and the superfluity is broken by these actions, and it heals through these actions.
However, this must be done near the time of evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
Of the cauterization of accidental blood flow by means of an archer's incision.
Many times there is an archer's flow [i.e., bleeding] that is incised towards the plague which emerges from the outside, or towards the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar things, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, if this happens to someone, then you press with your hand at the opening of the wound, and place your index finger over that, pressing repeatedly until the blood flow beneath your finger is stopped and no longer escapes.
And then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire, many of them, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is ruptured, and quickly place the cautery onto that vein *after* you have stopped the flow with your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is cauterized and stopped.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among the cauteries that have been prepared in the fire, and thus continue doing this repeatedly until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and cause further harm to the patient.
And understand that when blood flows from the artery, it cannot be stopped, especially if the artery is large, without one of four methods: either with a cautery as we have described, or by extraction if it is not already extracted (because when it is extracted, the patient's extremities are often damaged and the blood stops), or by tying it with thread or a ligature with a strong constriction, or by placing on it medicines whose properties are to constrict the blood and be pressed down with a cushion or tight wrapping.
However, those who administer constriction by tying and tightening with cloths or through the application of burning substances and similar methods will only be successful after a long time.
But if this happens to someone and the doctor is not present, nor is there medicine, then use the method of pressing a finger over the artery's opening as described, and maintain a firm pressure until the blood is stopped from its excessive flow, and the finger is not removed from the artery; then pour cold water of great coldness over the artery until the blood is congealed and thickened; and further action like cauterization or medical treatment should be used when needed.
Of incisions, perforations, leeches, phlebotomy, sores, and similar afflictions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all illnesses in which cauterization with fire is appropriate, along with burning medicines, and the occasions and causes of this, and the necessary instruments, with the forms of cauteries.  And I have ordered the chapters from the sovereignty of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker may be relieved of this, concerning whatever they have inquired about.
And before I begin this, children, you must understand that this chapter is a continuation of the first chapter on cauterization.
And therefore, great caution is necessary, for in the procedures of this chapter, often an evacuation of blood, which sustains life, is involved, such as the lancing of a vein, incision on an abscess, opening of a wound, healing of a sore, the extraction of an arrow, the incision of a stone, and similar procedures. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in their hands lies death.
And I warn you that this should not happen to you, for sometimes the hands of men bring many diseases. And some of them become enraged by their illness, and death is near to them, both because of the intensity of their illness, and because of the duration of their illness, and with the illness comes fear, which signifies death.
And of those is the one who gives you their possessions and says, "You, hoping for health, and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to one who comes to you with such a condition; and your caution should be greater than your eagerness and your rashness; and do not presume any cause regarding this except upon certain scientific knowledge which verifies this to you, and which leads to praiseworthy success.
And use in the treatment of all your patients prognostication and indication of the path towards their health, for in that lies your aid in gaining fame and memory, praise and glory.
Oh, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold direction and protection from you, for that is in his hand.
And I have organized this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, as preceding in the chapter on cauteries, from the treatment of the head to the feet, so that what you seek concerning this and what you need may be eased for you.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water that is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often comes to children during or after birth, and when the nurse tightens the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it arises from a hidden cause that is unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and all those I have seen have died, and thus it seems to me a hopeless case in them.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head of this child was always so enlarged that it could not hold itself upright because of the size of its head, and that the moisture increased until [the child] died.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or it is accumulated below the bone over the spinal column.
When the operation is performed in that case, if the moisture is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make a cut in the middle of the head with a lengthwise incision, and the length of the incision should be about two fingers until the moisture flows out.  This is the method of lancing.
And therefore, if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is below the bone, a sign of that is that you see the seams of the skull open on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press on it with your hand at different spots, and this is not hidden from you. You must cut in the middle of the head with three incisions in this way:
And then extract all the moisture from the incision; then compress the incisions with cloths and lint; then apply wine and oil to the wound until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with bandages and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry foods with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another way of incision, considering where the abscess's size and collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occipital area, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore make the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it in the manner that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow out and the patient die from that blood loss with the moisture evacuation.
Second Chapter.
When there is chronic catarrh or inflammation in the eyes or foot, and previous treatments with medicine have not worked, then the final treatment in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise those, where the patient's illness affects the head,  you should first rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the arteries, then firmly bind the patient's neck with a cloth; then consider where the vein pulsates; and the locations of each, which are near the ears, are depressed, and are somewhat hidden in some individuals.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and then cut each with a sharp-edged instrument, making an incision penetrating to the bone, and let this be done throughout the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the instrument beneath the artery and cut it at the incisions on the parts above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers wide, for when any vein is incised, the blood is emitted with a pulsating outflow, which leaps out in frequent spurts.
But if the artery is not visible, then you must measure from the ear a distance of three fingers, and then mark it with a probe, and cut into the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be drawn is about a wine-cupful, and one may draw more or less, depending on the condition of the patient and the fullness of the artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then dissect that so that a painful abscess does not develop.
And then staunch the wound with lint and linen bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When a person experiences a persistent headache or a sudden violent throbbing, due to acrid humidity and a quality in the temples' membranes, or is afflicted by a severe and similar ailment, and conventional medical treatments are ineffective, then we have found that in these illnesses, the extraction of arteries in the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have shown, is beneficial.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those [arteries] is that the patient shaves the hair from the temples; and is to locate the apparent artery in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except to a few because of its intensity.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's head should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply hot water until the artery is clearly visible. And then you take the instrument, of which this is the shape: And then you gently scrape the artery with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then, by applying pressure and extracting it in sections, you extract it from the sheath and expose the tissues underneath it, from every side.
But if the artery is delicate, then you wrap it with the tip of the instrument, and then cut off a portion according to the length that the parts are stretched and contracted, and no blood will flow, because if they are not cut off, the blood will not rise completely.
And then remove the blood from one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong double-fold linen or long cords, and do not allow it to fester before the wound heals, and excessive bleeding will result.
And then cut away the excess that is between the two ligaments; and do this now or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery instrument up to the bone, with a knife cautery until the ends are cut off, and it will remain in the same place.
And it is better if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot temperament; because cauterization is from among the things which help consume the humidity, and therefore it is better in youth.
And after the extraction of the arteries, you should fill the place with old cotton, and place well-made powders on it, and then treat it with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and with lotions until it is healed.
And if, while working, there is a flow of blood from the artery again, then you must try to stop the blood, either with a cautery or by filling the area with cotton and pressing your hand until the bleeding stops. And if none of those things is available to you, then place your finger on the area until the blood clots, and apply water of intense coldness to the area until the sharpness subsides, and tighten it as needed.
And the easiest way to extract an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two knives, since you have marked the two areas with a probe, and use each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between the ends is elongated, because it is not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives is equal. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives, it will cut close to the skin on each side, and won't cut through the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those areas, the heat of the fire is retained there and the flesh is separated easily.
And this method is better than any other method and easier.
And the space between the two knives should be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of bodies afflicted with sharp and acidic sores (or ulcers) of the humors.
When the sores are acidic, and the affected veins are on the scalp, and you find no medicinal cause for treatment, and sometimes the patient's face turns red and feels a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the sores are weakened, moist, so much so that their skin is exposed, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acidity of the tears, the ancient physicians used a particular treatment for these sores, and called this treatment "spassa".
And you command the patient to shave the hair from their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, according to the length of the forehead. And if the incision is two fingers long: the first incision should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a short distance along the path of the tear ducts that are in the lacrimal region, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries which are on either side, and keep your hand away from the continuing line between both jaws. And the space of each incision is the width of three fingers.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths, with which you absorb the blood.
And then place the spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of the incision on him: which is from the temple area up to the second incision. Scrape away with that all the membrane that is between the two incisions, using the instrument that is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision up to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that instrument against the flesh adjacent to the membrane, and the blunt edge on the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut off with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the heels.
But the incision should continue down to this part of the membrane, and then do this in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And since a quantity of congealed blood flows out, express the places of the clots of congealed blood.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old cloth, and place on that powders mixed in wine, oil, and sour wine, and be careful that it does not become a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basil ointment with rubbed oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the treatment of wounds by cauterization and ulcers descending from parts within the head.
When a sharp and persistent cauterization occurs to someone, and the patient's wounds are small and spreading, and the care of them is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and the skin around them affected, and there is sharp, piercing pain in the forehead area and consequent exhaustion, then know from these symptoms that the materials and cauterizations arise only from numerous veins in the inner parts.
And the best treatment in these cases is this:  command the patient to lie down, and then make a small incision in the middle of the forehead, in a straight line (starting with the incision from the left temple towards the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles, which are close to the chewing muscles), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or dry cloth, and then fill the gap between the two edges of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then cover it with powders mixed in wine and oil, so that a painful abscess does not develop.
And when you remove this and see that the abscess has begun to decrease, then it is necessary to rub the bone until flesh starts to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh, just as if you took two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony and mixed them together, and from this make a plaster and apply it to these wounds to aid in the growth of flesh.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of what ails the ears.
Every ailment in the ears is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron or glass; or it is from a plant, such as thistle or beans; or it is a flowing substance, like water, sour wine, and their likenesses; or some kind of beast.
And therefore, when a stone or similar hard substance is lodged in the ear, and you hold the ear towards the sun, and the stone is moved, then pour some oil of violets or wormwood into the ear, and then apply a method of extraction, and make the patient sneeze with some inducement, and block the nostrils in the course of the sneezing action. Then place some cloths or wool around the ear, and gently pull the ear upwards in parts, for often it is forcibly ejected by these methods.
And if it is not expelled, then apply an extraction method with an instrument called a *gesti epicicarios* (a fine, specialized extraction tool), of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the instrument, it is good; and if not, then apply the extraction method with a fine, little-folded (thin) probe.
And if it is not expelled by that, then make a brass cannula and insert the tip of the cannula into the ear canal, and seal around the cannula with softened wax and oil so that only the cannula remains open. And then extract with your breath with strong suction, for this often works as we have described.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a bright or previously prepared *halka* (likely a small, specialized instrument or bandage) of a sufficient size, and place this on the extremity of the wound, and then wrap a suitably placed piece of cloth around that. And then gently insert that into the ear canal, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then you must try to cut it out before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the way to cut it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to its proper virtue, and then place your hands around it and lift the ear outwards, and make a small incision in the root of the ear towards its base in the area well pressed by you. Let the incision be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone, and, upon finding the stone, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; and then quickly close the incision, and treat it until healed.
And if the cause festering in the ear is one of the growths that swell and become inflamed, then administer its cure and extraction; but if you do not succeed in this way, then you should take a fine, thin spatum (possibly a medicinal poultice or application) in this manner: And apply that to remove the growth that has festered in the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it has already been softened by the vapor from the ear, and place it in small pieces, and then carefully remove these with tweezers or with a delicate action, or with an incision as we have mentioned, for this will facilitate its removal.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing techniques, and fill their ear with some old cotton, and lie on the side with the affected ear down.  If the water is expelled this way, good.  If not, then take small, smooth, flat stones, the length of a finger, and gently heat them in a fire. The patient should then insert one of these stones into the ear canal, and rest their foot on that side, using another stone to tap the one inside the ear. So continue this with the stone until all the water has been expelled.
So continue this with the stone until all the water has been expelled. And once the water is expelled in this way, take some paper or linen, and place one end in the ear, while you hold the other end over a flame until the majority of it is burned.
Then put more linen in and repeat this process as many times as needed until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula, as described earlier regarding the removal of stones.
However, in removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, treat it like any other small foreign object; following the earlier instructions. But if it is large, and visible within the ear canal, then remove it with forceps or hooks.  This is a less problematic condition than most other ear obstructions.
But in removing a worm that has grown within the ear, if you treat it with the methods described in the sections on general treatment and distillations, and your treatment is unsuccessful, then you should expose the ear to sunlight.
And therefore, if any sign of a worm is visible, remove it with fine forceps or hooks.  But if no sign is visible, then take a cannula that is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, of which this is its form: Insert the narrow end of the cannula into the ear, according to the patient's tolerance; then, vigorously suction with it, repeating this many times until all the worms have been expelled.
But if this doesn't work, then you should seal the cannula's opening with wax, as previously described.  Then apply the distillations used by past practitioners in treating worms, which you will find in the section on distillations.
And use the method for pouring medicinal oils into the ear using this instrument, of which this is the shape: Make it of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom, with a small hole, and flat on top.
And therefore, it should be fitted so that the contents of the cannula's cavity are pressed against whatever is required. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap its end decently with cotton. Then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or the juice of some herb, or whatever medicine you choose, into the cannula (which is in the ear). Then insert the reed with the cotton on top and, with your hand, gently press it until the oil is pushed into the cavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil within.
And whatever is put into the ear should be warmed a little with fire. And be careful that the cause you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear blockages.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' wombs without being pierced, and this also happens to some men, a blockage in the ear canal due to an infection or some growing flesh there; and this blockage is sometimes at the opening of the ear canal itself which does not allow passage, and sometimes it is in the areas outside.
And that which happens at the opening of the ear, which does not allow passage, is in the greatest positions for difficult healing.
And in that which allows passage, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And therefore, if blockage appears, open it with a very thin instrument whose shape is like this:
If its extremity, in which the width is small, has one part sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin strings so it doesn't damage the ear.
And therefore, if the blockage is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove it with fine tweezers, and cut away that flesh vigorously until all of it is removed.
And if the blockage is in the front of the ear, then take a very thin, sharp reed, heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have perceived a blockage that the patient finds difficult to hear, relieve the pressure gently; and be careful not to injure the ear's nerves.
And then place a cloth over the ear, the size of the hole you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing; or take some and grind it into powder over the wound, and apply it; and if the ear has already become festering, then you must remove the poultice and replace it with another poultice wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the inflammation subsides; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it over the ear, and use other treatments that constrict the bleeding, which are described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling that appears on the eyelids.
Swellings that appear on the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes are dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and separate them from their roots with a spatula, and then apply a compress to the areas.
But if pain with a flow of blood occurs, then you cauterize them with a thin, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains of their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often, in the eyelids of the eyes, there is some cause that is like a swelling in its strength and hardness, and therefore it is called swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the operation for them is to consider whether the swelling is protruding from the eyelid and spreading in all directions; and the cause of it is slight.
Therefore, make a slit across it, and then cut it away from all sides with ease until it opens, and then lift it with forceps and cut it.
And if the figure [or, swelling] is large, then you should close it with sutures; and heal it until it is cured.
And if it is small, you will not experience any harm because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the size [of the swelling] is decreasing towards the inner parts of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and hold the swelling with forceps, instead of incising it, and press it on all sides. And if the eyelid is stretched by the incision, that does not matter in any way.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and heal the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a tumor on the eyelid above the eye.
A tumor is a serious condition that is generated in the tissues of the eyelid above the eye, and it is more common in children. It aggravates the eyes of those affected and causes them to have difficulty seeing clearly in the morning; and they cannot look at the sunlight because of it, as tears accumulate in those affected.
And because of this, you often see those lying on their faces all the time, sleeping on their faces; and there are moist spots under the eyelids, on their eyebrows, which are already covered by inflammation, and the appearance of a tumor is evident in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the tumor there.
And a certain method of operation is that the patient rests their head on your lap, and you then place a cloth over them and make a circle around the tumor, encompassing it entirely.  Then place this over the affected area and press with your fingers so the tumor is contained within the circle. Then, in the center of this moisture, make a small incision with a sharp instrument, and the incision should not be larger than that made in a standard surgical procedure.
But in the process, you must cut through the skin entirely until you reach the tumor, for in most cases, the tumor is spread by a white, greasy substance.
Therefore, remove that [the white, greasy substance] with cloths that you have wrapped around it, between your fingers, and twist it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful not to force the incision, so that with the instrument you don't pierce the eye and damage it.
But if the tumor doesn't appear at the beginning of the incision, but rather you need to widen the incision slightly to make the tumor appear; and then remove it as previously described; and then immerse cloths in warm water and vinegar, and place them over the area, and press them down with powders.
And some [doctors/practitioners] sprinkle salt and place it in the wound cavity to absorb any remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing poultices until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Methods of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural skin and they are hardened, then these irritate the eye and cause various ailments such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and tissues, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called "tactinir of the eye."  It is treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or with a sharp medicine as previously described in the chapter on cauterization; or a "tactinir" is treated by incision and suture; or by means of a canula (small tube), as I will describe.
And therefore you should place the patient's head on your lap; then, grasp the eyelid with your left hand, and if it is already turned back, good.  But if not, insert a needle into the thickened part, below the eyelid, and thrust the needle with the thread above, and this should be—near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow—close to the hairs. Pull the thread towards the upper eyelid and then pull the eyelid back into position with your hand (or a tool).
Then, in the part inside the eyelid beneath the hardened hairs, make incisions with the major lacrimal (tear duct) extending down to the minor lacrimal; then withdraw the thread and place small, powdered cotton or cloth beneath the eyelid.  Then cover the eyelid with a bandage, in a shape resembling a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape of the bandage should correspond to the degree of eyelid swelling, since this varies from person to person.  Some patients require a more extensive incision in the eyelid, depending on how much the eyelid has swollen.
Then, with the spatula, make incisions along the two lines you have drawn, starting from the major lacrimal duct and extending towards the minor lacrimal duct. And if your incision is close to the natural hairs, it should be as wide as the king's finger.
Then place the lancet at one of the two corners of the wound, and scrape it all.  Then use a thin wool thread or thread to draw together the two lips of the wound, and remove the blood. Apply any excess thread to the two eyebrows if you wish; otherwise, don't worry about it.
And leave the stitches of the thread for three or four days, then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches and treat it with what dries and constricts it, since the eyelid is raised against the sealing of the wound and its adherence; however, stitching is better.  Therefore, the ancient physicians said this.
But nevertheless, there should be concern for the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear for them.
And there is another method in treating this, which is to draw on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three separated and joined hooks according to this shape:
But if that is not possible, to hold the hooks, and it is not appropriate, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads equally between these, and then, with a firm upward motion, elevate the entire flap, on which the figure was drawn, as we have described.
And then stitch together the two edges of the wound, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess appears near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with poultices and similar remedies, and with ointments.
And sometimes the eyelashes on the eyelid become inverted; then you should use what we have said about incisions, but a suture is often more effective/better in such cases.
To treat with canes (likely referring to a type of medical treatment using canes or rods) is according to this method: And you must turn the eyelid and cut the figure within as I have described; and then, skillfully, make two thin canes or rods, whose length is the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the opening (space). And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads (or sutures).
Then carefully attach each one to the excess eyelid tissue, and tightly bind the canes to each part. Leave it for several days, because the excess skin, due to the tightening, will die and become withered and decayed until it falls off naturally.
However, if it has lingered, then break it open with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Thus, once it is healed, the eyelid is elevated and the skin is not inflamed.
And this is the method for two canes, which you will do according to this method: and have the quantity of the length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of raising inflamed skin on the boil with a needle when the skin is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and inflamed boils, then the operation for them is that you take a fine needle and place it on that delicate and soft part of the boil, and then you adjust the ends of it, twisting each one in a very subtle manner. And then you place another thin thread underneath it shortly, and twist the ends of that one if you want.

Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a wound that appears on the upper eyelid.
When a wound appears in the eye, and the eyelid is contracted upwards, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, a cut, or cauterization, and is called 'leporine' (meaning hare-lip-like), this wound or ulcer may be either natural or accidental, and is caused by poor healing from a wound, a cut, or cauterization, or similar poorly treated injuries.
The method of treatment is to dissect the scar tissue and separate the edges, placing a piece of linen cloth between them, and binding them together until they heal.
And you should not use treatments that dry or constrict, for if you do this, the wound will return worse than it was before any treatment.
Use remedies that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice made from water with chamomile and flaxseed, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil, and immerse the cloth in it, and treat with that.
The key to your treatment is that you should perforate any unhealed portion according to the original form of the wound.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing more than a rectification of the wound, restoring the area to its previous shape.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a wound made on the lower eyelid.
This wound on the lower eyelid, truly called a wound, can be either natural or accidental in origin.

And the way to treat it is to take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and have it pass from the tear duct on the left side to the tear duct on the right side, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh apart with the needle and sew it together with the needle, and cut away with a wide spatula. And then remove the flesh in an amount equal to the sharpness of the lower eyelid, which is what forms the eyelid.

Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.

Chapter 25.
Of ulcers in the nasal extremities.
Often an ulcer forms at the extremities of the nose, and it grows and enlarges in a few days until it is very difficult to see.
And therefore, you should cut it open at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then place either a cautery or a burning medicine on the area.
However, if its incision is delayed until it has grown large, then consider if it is hardened, hard, dark-colored, and with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron, for it is a chronic abscess; for often the one who incises this abscess can cause great harm to the person it was in.
And if the abscess is tender to the touch and not dark-colored, and its incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with things that dry and constrain it until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by plague or similar [diseases/wounds].
Know that when you encounter a disruption of continuity in one of these cartilages, you will achieve little in that operation except in skilled hands.
Therefore, when you encounter any such cause, consider if it is fresh plague with its own blood.  If so, you should bring together the two sides of the wound with sutures and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the broken continuity are already separated and each is apparently healthy, then you must scrape each part of their edges until blood comes out. Then bring together the two edges with sutures and tighten them.  Powder the area with dragon's blood and crushed herbs, and place a piece of diaphanous plaster or another of the consolidating plasters upon the powder.  Leave this dressing for two or three days, and then remove it and change the plaster, leaving it until the pus is separated, and then treat with unguent until healed.
But the method of suturing is to join the broken continuity either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the belly, or join the break with glue, as you have been shown.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that are inside the two edges/lips of the wounds.
Often, in the inner parts of their lips, men have some hard abscesses resembling grains of corn, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips and slit each of those hardnesses from every side, and then fill the wound with scraped sage until the blood is stopped, and then wash the area with sour wine and treat the area with something astringent, until it heals.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, flesh adhering to the gum, which the ancients called "albulus," forms a growth.  Then, you must detach it with a knife or forceps, and cut it at its base, allowing the pus or blood to drain.
And then apply crushed sage or other astringent or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again and cauterize it, for the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
About toothache with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, there are rough, hard, and sometimes black or yellowish scabs, which cause the gum to become inflamed and the teeth to become hard.
And therefore, you should place the patient between your hands, place their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth where those scabs and similar things like sand appear until no trace of them remains, and do this to remove the blackness, yellowness, and other such things until they are cleaned.
Therefore, if what is described at the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those areas the following day, and the second, and the third, until it reaches the desired outcome.
And know that teeth require many tools of various shapes and forms, depending on the preparation for your operation. And therefore, the scraper used to clean the inner portion of the tooth is different from the scraper used to clean between the teeth, in different ways, and these different scrapers must be prepared by you, and you will see them soon.
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You must treat the tooth's pain with all your skill and take your time in any tooth extraction, because there is no remedy to replace the tooth once it is removed, for it is a sublimated substance until it is extracted.
However, when the patient must have a tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is the painful one, because many times the pain deceives the patient and indicates a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, for we have seen this in the practice of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which tooth is painful, and that you know it well?  Then you must scrape the gum around the tooth with a substance that has some virtue, until it is loosened from the tooth and gum on all sides, and then work it with your fingers, or with small, sharp scissors, little by little, until it loosens.
And then, use strong pliers for good grip or strong leverage, and place the patient's head between your knees to hold it firmly so it doesn't move, and then pull the tooth straight, in a way that does not break it, and do not cause the patient excessive pain.
And so, if it is not completely loosened, then take one of those instruments and place it under it from all sides with ease and exert the removal of it just as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you must cover the crack with cloth and tighten it with the fine end of a thin rod, so that it is not broken during the application of pliers to remove it.
And you must carefully scrape around the gum on all sides, and be very careful to avoid breaking it and damaging any part, and return to the patient, for the treatment's severity may exceed the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their recklessness and audacity in tooth extraction, without the care we have described; for many times they bring upon people serious illnesses that are more difficult to cure than the original, leaving the roots of the tooth entirely, or some of them, or even tearing off some bone from the jaw as we have often witnessed.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be rinsed with warm wine or vinegar with salt.
And if blood flows from this, as often happens, then apply a small amount of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if that does not stop it, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers with which the tooth is initially grasped should be long at the end, with a short and thick handle so that they do not bend when using them.
And they should be like those with thick handles so that when you grip them, they don't bend, but with short ends.
And they should be made of well-forged Indian iron with straight ends, and on those ends are teeth that interlock and grip tightly with strong constriction. Sometimes the ends of these are shaped like the beak of a stork (or, likely, a similar bird of prey). These too have a strong grip.
Chapter 31.
Of the eradication of teeth and the extraction of bones from fractured jaws.
When you must eradicate a fractured tooth which remains for some reason, then you should place wool with a bandage on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then use forceps or pliers whose ends are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork or heron, the shape of forceps:
The ends of them [the forceps] should be made like a file or rasp.
