And it should be of short length, with a thick end, a little bit, and not overly sharp so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is completely loosened, good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the shapes: the shape of the first instrument is triangular at the end, with some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is to be triangular and with a more slender end.
And you have now been assisted by this instrument having two ramps, of which this is the shape, approximately the following: and with other instruments, which I will describe in the rasping of teeth; for helpfulness is done with this instrument, which is like a large chisel, and this is the form:
of a triangular, bent or short end, which has some thickness, and should not be blunt so that it is not chipped.
And further know that the instruments for teeth are very similar to the other instruments.
But the skillful and subtle craftsman in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and diverse illnesses indicate to him; for there are illnesses for which the ancients did not devise or prescribe a specific instrument, because of the diverse shapes of illnesses.
However, if the jawbone or other bones of the mouth are fractured, or decayed, then apply a compress to it in its place with what is appropriate to it from the instruments and forceps that we have mentioned in the extraction of teeth roots, and help with a gesture of which this is the form:
And if there is some small thickness, it should be pressed or held in the mouth so that it does not escape or loosen until the bone is completely set; and afterward restore the area with suitable remedies for this.
But if there is a small ulcer on the bone, then cut away that ulcer and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth prominent above the others.
When teeth are prominent beyond their natural position, due to an irregular shape, especially in women, and more so in young women, then you must consider: if a tooth is directly next to another tooth, and removal or binding is not possible, then cut it out; but if it's not attached to another tooth, then you cut it with an instrument shaped like a pig's snout, made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And make your incision in such a way that you cut it in several days, because of the tooth's hardness, and so that it doesn't damage other teeth.
And if a prominent tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file, of which this is the shape:
And it should be entirely of Indian iron, easily worked; and it should have many small points, like the file used to make needles, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days easily, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then, finally, bind it and pull it out with some razors.
And if a tooth has a broken part that cuts the tongue, then you must file it until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and made whole so that the tongue is not harmed and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver fillings.
When teeth are damaged by impact or displacement due to a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot firmly hold on to what's missing, and you've treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment hasn't worked, then, because it won't hold, fix those that are broken with gold fillings and not silver, for silver turns green and will become rotten after a few days, but gold never changes.
And the filling should be of such a fineness and thickness as to fit between the teeth; and the method of applying it is to take the filling and place twice its size between two healthy teeth, and then work it in with two bends of the filling between the broken teeth, or if there is one or more, until the material reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the process until you reach the starting point, and apply firm but gentle pressure until it is no longer broken.
And your grip, with which you hold the filling against the tooth roots, must be firm to prevent it from coming out.
And then cut off the excess parts of the filling with forceps or scissors, and shape them to fit each location, and position them between the healthy tooth and the broken tooth, so as not to injure the tongue. And then leave it like that, firmly in place.
But if it is loose or broken, hold it or them in place with another filling in such a manner at all times.
And this is the method for treating teeth, and the procedure for joining two healthy teeth to two broken teeth, as these teeth are sometimes joined together:
And when you have returned a tooth or two to their place, close up each one, and they should have grown back as we have described, and remain in place.
And only skilled and knowledgeable craftsmen can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow's bone is suitable, shaped like a tooth, and placed in the space where the tooth is missing, and held in place as we said, and it remains and serves well for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the inflammation of the ligament that affects the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament, which sometimes forms beneath the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has already healed.
And the procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, their head being supported by you; lifting their tongue, and then carefully cutting the nerve ligament along its width until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting in that area, a result of a healed wound, then you apply leeches and cut it along its width until you have severed the ligament and dissolved the knot.
And be careful not to create a fissure in the flesh, and to avoid cutting into a blood vessel and causing bleeding.
Then wash the incision site with water or vinegar or cold water; and then place a linen pad under the tongue; this is a bandage that the patient will keep under their tongue every night to prevent it from sticking together again.
However, if bleeding occurs, apply powdered vitriol to the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a smooth cautery iron suitable for this purpose. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula (a cyst) beneath the tongue.
Sometimes, an abscess resembling a small tumor forms beneath the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the treatment for this is to open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is a dark color, or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that, for it is cancer.
And if it is pale, with moisture, then apply a poultice to it, and cut it with a thin scalpel, and separate it from all sides; and if blood is released during your operation, then place a crushed piece of gypsum, or vitriol, on top to stop the bleeding.
And then return to your work until you have successfully removed it, rinsing it with vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses from the two ears and those on the throat and other abscesses.
When a boil develops on the throat, which are glands clustered together, which burst outward and are called boils, and if you treat them with what we've described in the division and they don't heal, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and has little sensitivity. In that case, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is reddish in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for fear of blood flow; rather, leave it until it is ripe, and then either you will drain it or it will break open by itself.
And if it is whitish, soft, and its root is subtle, then it is appropriate to make an incision.
And the procedure for it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already been warmed with adequate rest or if it has diminished with some reduction; and then make the patient sit in the sun, and place their head on your lap, open their mouth, and the servant taking hold of their hands, press down on the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, whose form is as follows:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be thin like a knife.
So, when you press with that on the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, and you cover it with your cloth, then take a spoon and place it in the cavity, and pull it out to the sides as much as you can. After that, if you pull out any cause of the tissues, then carefully cut it with an instrument whose shape is this:
After that, if you pull out any cause of the tissues, then carefully cut it with an instrument whose shape is this: and it is similar to forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is on the opposite side, very sharp, and made from tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a spatula that has this shape:  And it should be sharp on one side, and blunt on the other.
And after you have incised one incision, make another incision in the same manner, and then the patient's incision will be healed by cold water.
However, if there is bleeding, heal it with water decoction of pomegranate bark or myrtle leaves, and similar styptic substances until the bleeding is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the neck or near the two ears, and cut those according to the incision of two incisions previously described.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her neck, an abscess that was tending toward a dark color, with little sensitivity, which had already constricted her throat, and the woman was breathing with difficulty, and I had forbidden her food and water, and she was close to death if it had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had swollen so much that it had formed two bulges before bursting through two openings.
Then I incised it fistularly and placed a cupping instrument on one of them, and pulled it out, and a large portion was pulled out.  Then I incised the area where I understood another opening was, and then opened the patient's mouth, and pressed down on their tongue, and then inserted the cupping instrument into that abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon ate food.  So I continued to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess would form in the same place, which I incised until it resolved.
Therefore, I cauterized the throat, adding more cautery; and then I pondered and did not know what God had wrought upon her.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of an abscess when it is inflamed.
When catarrh settles on the uvula, and it is inflamed and prolonged, then it is called a perpendicular abscess; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a boil.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, and sometimes an abscess has already subsided and is becoming more subtle, then you should incise that which is round and swollen; and if it extends in length, and is blood-red, dark-colored, or black, and there is no feeling there, then you should hesitate to incise it, for there is fear for the patient's well-being in that case.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, you should have the patient lie down and press the tongue with an instrument that reveals its shape. Then, position the lancet on the swelling, and draw it down and incise it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the incision of double boils.
And you should only incise that which is adjacent to the natural boundary, without exceeding it, for if you incise further, you will damage the patient's speech.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of double boils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, use cauterization without hesitation.  The method of cauterization in this case is only with sharp water.  You should position the patient's head on your lap, and then press the tongue with the instrument we have mentioned.  Then take the water described in the chapter on cauterization, apply it in a live, non-thick, non-thin layer.  Fill the hollow of the instrument which has this form:
The tip of it (the instrument) should be concave, like the bowl of a spoon. Place the instrument with the medicine on the ulcer.  The patient should lie on their side, so that saliva or mucus from the mouth flows away from the medication, preventing any substance from running down the patient's throat and irritating it.
Then, place your hand with the medicine and press it onto the uvula for half an hour, until you see it blackened and softened by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take cotton, wrap it around the tip of the spoon, dip the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a tube until the cotton adheres to the tube. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it until it dries and falls away in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it. Afterward, clean the cauterized area around the uvula with cotton dipped in water or a similar liquid.  Remove any remaining medicine. Wash the area with cool water. Treat it externally with an ointment, and internally with remedies, until it is healed.
This type of treatment is safer than an incision and less prone to fear (or complications).
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more delicate than cauterization or incision, through fumigation in this way: take mint, hyssop, rue, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, balm, and similar herbs, combine some or all in a pot, and immerse them in sour wine and boil.  The pot should be properly sealed, and a small hole is to be made in the middle of the pot over which a concave metal instrument, either silver or bronze, is placed in this manner.
And the end of the instrument is inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is significantly softened.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry.  Be careful not to perform this treatment at the start of an abscess, as it would only make the abscess worse; you should instead treat an abscess with heat (cautery).
However, if you do not have this instrument, take a reed and place a hard-boiled egg shell on its end, so that the patient's mouth is protected, as the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the removal of thorns and things that adhere to the throat and other such causes.
Many times, thorns or fish bones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Then it is necessary for you to remove whatever of those is apparent, upon which you fix your gaze, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be revealed to you what is in the throat.
And that which is not apparent and is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause adhering to the throat will be easily expelled by vomiting; or that the patient swallows a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or that the patient takes a piece of dry sea sponge and tightly binds it with a bandage, and when the patient swallows it, because it reaches the place where the thorns are and is expelled, then it is expelled. And further, take an eel and drink it with a bandage, and it should be placed inside and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly put it in and quickly take it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
And it should be larger than a small ray, and at its end should be a cavity which you easily insert into its throat.
And have the patient hold their head up, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not hurt, and with that you push on the bone or thorn, or the physician with their hand.
But it is better if the patient, by their own knowledge of the place of the associated cause, pushes it down, and the doctor pulls their hand with the instrument upwards, all of this according to the amount prepared for them, until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the removal of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When the leech is medicated with what we have described in the division of treatment with medicine, and you have not succeeded, then place the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed the tongue with the instrument we have discussed.
Therefore, if you see the leech, remove it with a small forceps or with subtle, skillful gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's throat. Then, place a heated iron in the concavity of the tube, and do this repeatedly (until the leech is dead and detached from the throat).  And keep the patient abstaining from water all day.
Then take a vessel full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it.  And let him not swallow anything, and hold the water for an hour or so with your hand, because the leech will immediately fall off when it senses the water.
But if it is not dislodged by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with wormwood or asafoetida using the instrument we described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it falls off.
And another method of fumigation for this is to take a pan in which there are burning coals, and the pan is covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole.  Place the end of the instrument in this hole.  Then, perform the fumigation above it.  Have the patient put their mouth over the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will fall off immediately.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and let them eat salty foods and garlic, and do not let them drink water; and thus, with this regimen, it is necessary that it be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, but differs in that it has some curvature in it. Which is placed on the leech, and its tip is similar to a crane's beak, which is covered, and in which a file is placed, so that when we grasp any thing, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and the incision of them.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to their remembrance, each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and by what we grasp of the humours.
And the second way is according to the locations on the body where they occur, for an abscess in the head is different from an abscess in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy area is different from an abscess in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment regarding treatment.
And some abscesses should not be opened until the pus is sufficiently mature or the abscess is fully formed.
Other abscesses should be incised while they are still inflamed, and not fully mature, such as those near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its presence often extends until it impacts the surrounding area, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are easily corrupted, causing contraction of that limb; or it is near a vital limb, and if you delay its opening until it matures, you risk harming the limb; or it's near the anus, therefore open it while unripe.  Do not open an unripe, incompletely formed abscess, but if it's near the anus,  so that the pus does not spread and extend into the depths of the anus, creating a fistula, or causing a lasting lesion that will not heal. But you must know the right time to open the abscess, and this is when it is mature.
And you will know it has matured when the pain subsides and the fever ceases, and when the swelling and throbbing decrease, and the head of the abscess becomes prominent, and the other signs.
And therefore, the incision should be made in the pus-filled area beneath the abscess, and from the pus coming from the swelling.
And the incision should always be made along the grain of the flesh or the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, nerves, and arteries; and, in general, in all places where there is no reflex action; for in those areas that have a reflex, use the incision appropriate for that particular limb.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to let it be until the digestion of the area is confirmed according to the fulfillment of the condition, as we have said; for if you incise it before this, you prolong the illness and much filth, and the lips of the abscess will harden and deepen.
But some abscesses are incised according to the width of the flesh or body, depending on the necessity, or according to the needs of the limb.
And you should make a small incision in small abscesses, and a large and wide incision, or many sections, in large abscesses, according to the variation in size of the abscesses.
And others are of abscesses in which the skin should be hollowed out and incised, when it is done in such a way that it is in a state of maturity, such as those that occur in cases of many boils and buboes.
And some are incised with a three-angled section; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the groin.
And of those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are used, and similar to those.
But in those cases where there is no such deep abscess, like superficial lion abscesses, you should pierce them with a superficial, or flat, incision only.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it and do not attempt to remove all the matter at once; having removed a portion, you should then bandage the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and do this repeatedly according to the degree, until it is entirely discharged.
And especially if the patient is of frail constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old person, because the animal spirit is often dissolved by the discharge of pus, and the patient dies easily, and you should be careful with your treatment.
Observe great caution in this chapter, and when making your incision into these abscesses, you should cleanse the wounds and consider whether the abscess wound is small, or a flat incision. If so, apply lint or old cloth. But if the abscess is large and the openings of the incision are many, you should place medicaments in each hole until they meet.
If in the abscess a portion of the skin has been incised or has been hollowed out around it, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made of linen, like when you have removed bread or roots, without moisture.  And bind that tightly until the third day. Then proceed with treatment and heal it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
But if you encounter a blood flow during your procedure, then apply cold water and vinegar on a linen cloth, and place that over the site of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer treatment and remedies, using the powders described in many places in this book and in related sections.
But if it is wintertime, and the site of the abscess is a region with many nerves, then you must infuse powders in warm wine and oil, and apply those to the affected areas.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must apply powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all of this should be cool until the third day, as we have said. Then you must drain the abscess, cleanse it, and provide appropriate treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary to know about the treatment of abscesses in general; however, according to the method of distinguishing that I have already described, the treatment of each type of abscess is detailed in specific chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that appear on the scalp.
Small abscesses appear on the scalp, and they are a type of swelling containing membranes that are formed around them; and their variety is quite significant.
And some contain pus, and from that comes a moisture resembling lime. And some contain a moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and some are like stones, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as extracting them does not cause any counter-reaction or harm.
And the surgical procedure for incising them is that you probe them with an instrument shaped like this, called an *entromes*, until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then you cut it with an incision along the length, by a superficial incision, like this:
And begin the incision on line B up to line G.  Then, once the moisture is evacuated, you scrape off the skin containing that moisture, and leave absolutely nothing of it, for it often returns if any remains.
And then soak a cloth in Egyptian unguent, if available, and otherwise in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it for another day, because this will corrode any remaining skin fragments.
And then repeat applying the Egyptian unguent to that cloth a second and a third time if needed, until you are confident that no remnants of that skin remain; then treat the wound with unguent until healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you incise it in this way: with an incision shaped like a cross, and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in removing the membrane that contains it.
But if your effort encounters resistance, then do what I have told you first.
And similarly, do in the calcified abscess the incision and treatment as we have described; and incision on a calcified abscess is easier, because it is of little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses that are on the head in some men lack moisture; the reason for this is that I incised an abscess on the head of an elderly woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone were to be struck with that, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses on the head, this is not the case, like those abscesses that arise near the roots of the ears in children, but the incision of all these should be superficial.
And make the perforation in those from the lower part, so that the body is relieved of the blood in the lower parts.
And then treat them with what is suitable for their treatment.
Chapter 42.
Of the incision of scrofulas which occur on the neck.
Often these abscesses appear on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.

Therefore, in the one that you find well-disposed upon touch, of which the apparent color is similar to that of a stone, and it is soft in every part, and is not connected to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the arteries, and is not deep, you should incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, along a line from G to B, and drain it thoroughly, and spread the edges of the stone with forceps, or with several forceps if necessary, as we said in the case of head abscesses, and extract that little by little.
And it is more important that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And if the pustule is not very sharp, do not press on it with your incision, or disturb the patient, and do not cut what is not necessary to cut; for if you should cut a vein or artery and thus hinder your operation, then apply crushed vitriol or other powders that stop bleeding, and compress the area, and leave it until the severity of the abscess subsides and the affected area softens and begins to decompose. When the blood is drawn.

Of the incision of the boil of an abscess that has happened inside the throat.

Chapter XLIV.
Of incision on an abscess that occurs in the throat, outside, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess, called a throat hernia, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body, and it is common in women.
And it is in two ways, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural [case], there is no way to cure it.
And the accidental [case] is in two ways, of which one is similar to fatty tumors, and the other way is similar to an abscess caused by an artery blockage, and in that case there is fear (of complications). Chapter 45.
Of the incision of the types of tumors.
There are many types of tumors, and I have already described all their types in the division.
And it is necessary that I recount the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as there might be doubt.
Therefore, I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the boiling of the excess is quelled; and complete putrefaction occurs, at which time the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a tumor there is no heat, fever, or pain, and it resembles that lion's paw which is on the proper sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a seed, and it is made thus, like a vessel, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for either it is fatty and contains moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the cure for a tumor, you should examine it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument called a probe, as its form will be shown in the chapter that follows this one.
And it is a way of investigating abscesses and all tumors, and it is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most moist place you find in the abscess, and you will turn your fingers with that little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin.
Then, finally, place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then extract the probe and consider what has come out on the trace of it.
And if the flowing moisture, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you dissect it with a superficial incision as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if from the trace of the probe there is no moisture, then know that it is fat.
And dry it with an incision having the shape of a cross, in this manner: As I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and carefully scrape the pus from all sides, and if possible, save the skin or sac over it and remove the matter with a knot.
However, if it breaks during the operation, it is not possible to remove all the matter, and often this happens, and then you remove it in pieces until nothing remains.
For if any part, little or much, remains, the knot will return to the affected area.
And if you see any small part remaining, then fill the wound with some sharp, corrosive powders, and compress the wound, and place a warm poultice over it, and treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large knot, then you sew the two edges together and treat it with something that promotes healing.
But if there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein nearby, then you cut and fill the area with crushed vitriol (green vitriol), and leave it in place for two or three days until the wound subsides and the blood's inflammation quiets down; and then return to dissecting out what remains of the knot.

Chapter 49.
Of the incision of abscesses caused by arrow wounds or by vein damage.
When an arrow wound is inflicted, and the sheath surrounding it is consolidated, often an abscess arises. And the one caused by a vein is round and evident on the body's surface.
And incision on this abscess is a mistake, especially in those located under the armpits, in the elbows, in the neck, and many other places on the body; and it is very serious, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that on the extremities, or head, should be avoided.
But on those caused by inflammation of the arrow's opening, make an incision along the sheath; and then widen the incision with hooks and pull out the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is exposed.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and pull the arrow with a double thread at two points—which I teach you in the extraction of two arrows, which are in the Templars.
And then prick the area between the two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.

Chapter 1 of the abscess that arises from a twisting of the nerves.

Chapter 52.
Of the treatment of a swelling at the navel.
A swelling at the navel is caused by various things; either by ruptures in the tissue over the belly, where part of the bowel or intestine is protruding, like other hernias; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as mentioned before; or by trapped air or gas in that location.
However, if it's due to a rupture in the abdominal wall and protrusion of the tissue, then the color of the abscess will resemble the color of the body; and it is painless, and appears in various locations. And if it's due to the intestine protruding, then its location is different from what we have described, with more diverse symptoms, and when you press on it with your fingers, it is hidden and then returns; and it's very close to the opening of the abdomen, and greatly swollen and very painful.
But if it's due to moisture, then it's painless, and it is not hidden when you press with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor swollen.
And if it's due to blood, then the abscess appears with a darkening, blackish coloration.
But if it's due to dead flesh, then the abscess is severe, large, and hard, and is fixed in a specific area.
But if it's due to wind (gas), then the touch of it is painless.
And the treatment for it is to consider whether the swelling near the navel is from an arrow wound, a vein injury, or wind/gas; then you should avoid treating it, because there is fear and dread in such cases, as has been taught in the chapter where I discussed abscesses from arrow wounds and vein injuries.
And if the swelling near the navel is due to the intestine or the mesentery, then you should tell the patient to hold their breath and lie stretched out.  Then, gently probe the area around the entire navel with a probe.  Then, have them face between your hands on their back and carefully use a blunt instrument to circle the area around the navel where you marked with the probe.  Then, carefully cut away half of the abscess at the top portions with a large scalpel.  Then, tie the area around the perimeter with strong thread or linen and tie in a proper, well-constructed manner.
Then, cut open the middle of the enlarged abscess over the ligament and insert your finger into that opening and search for the intestine.  And if you have found or identified the intestine within the ligament, then you gently extract and push the intestine back into the abdominal cavity.
And if you have found the mesentery, then take hold of it with a scalpel and cut away the excess.
However, if it is an arrow wound or vein injury, then encircle that area and then return to your procedure.  Take two needles and thread strong thread through them, and insert the needles into the circle you created around the abscess in a cross pattern, pushing them in. Then, compress the abscess into four sections over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the swollen flesh subsides and falls away on its own; or, cut it open when it is softened and begins to yield, and then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling at the navel is caused by flesh growing there or by moisture, then it is necessary to drain the abscess as we have described and remove the blood or moisture you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate to consolidate the area.

And if it is difficult to discern them precisely, then you should scrape off any cause of adhesion between the head of the penis and the foreskin, and this is because the foreskin, when thin, is easily pierced by its own thinness.
Then separate the foreskin from the head of the penis with thin linen cloths, which have already been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
And then it should be treated with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter 57.
Of the purification of children and the treatment of what occurs in those undergoing circumcision.
Circumcision is not a simple break in the skin like other wounds; however, since it is our procedure and of our volition, and its purpose is primarily in children, it is necessary to focus on the best procedure and easiest path to recovery.

And I have already tried all these methods and have found no better purification with scissors, with a thread ligature, than with purification with a fingernail, because many times the foreskin comes off too quickly, as the foreskin has two layers, and the deeper layer remains, and it is easily forced to further incision and a more certain pain. And with purification that is performed with a knife, and is digital, which cobblers use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secure, for it easily enters the opening.
But in the procedure with a fingernail, it is very easy to damage the iron and spoil your work. And the foreskin of the child is short by nature, and many times they are born in such a way that it does not require purification, and I have seen that.
But the purification done with scissors and a thread ligature, its goodness, experience has shown me, because the scissors are for a proportional incision, because the sharpness on top is as sharp as the sharpness below; and thus when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportional, and you can cut it according to a comparison in a moment; and the compression of the thread, in a form containing the foreskin of the penis on all sides, prevents any error altogether.
And the first method of the operation is that you should have the child mostly if it is one that understands a little, and you should simply tie the thread around its penis and leave it until the next day.
And then do that joyfully in whatever way possible for that which receives its intelligence. And then place that between your hands, firmly prepared, and let there be no hesitation; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so the child does not see them or any other instrument.
And then put your hand on the penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is exposed, and then clean it of any dirt clinging to it.
The shape of the scissors for cleaning, this is the shape:
One end should be blunt and the other sharp, so the blade doesn't come out of the handle, and the length of both blades should be the same as the length of two handles together.
But the error in cleaning is that you forcefully remove the foreskin completely, or part of it, from the incision.
Therefore, you must extend that with your fingertip before the abscess is incised and make the incision along the line of equal length.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingertip, then pull it out with tweezers and make the incision.
But if three days have passed and that which is inflamed beneath the penis remains an abscess, then you leave it until the hot abscess has subsided, and easily scrape it away, and then make the incision as you have prepared.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for that will not help him.
And then cure it with what consolidates wounds from the powders that we have described in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised from the tendon excessively and you make incisions on the surrounding areas, that will not benefit him.
And further treat it with what we have said until it is healed.

Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and stones formed in the bladder, and the signs of each.

And the stone formed in the bladder is properly that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear explanation and brevity.
Therefore, I say that stones formed in the bladder primarily affect children; and among the signs of it is that the urine expelled from the bladder is like water in its subtlety, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs their penis, frequently groans, and the penis is often erected; and with this they strain their anus in many of them.
And the cure is easier in children than those who have reached fourteen years of age; and it is a difficult cure in the elderly. But in young people it is easier because it is among them (or: it is common among them).
And for that in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning to clean the patient with a clyster that removes all the inflammation in their bowels; for this inflammation sometimes hinders the expulsion of the stone from its place.
And then the patient should be placed with their feet elevated and gently beaten/massaged below so that the stone descends to the bladder, or that it jumps out of its place, or sometimes.
And then, position the patient with their hands placed beneath their thighs, so that the entire bladder is positioned downward.
And then, remove that (the stone) and all the rest from outside (the body).
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you immediately apply a cut (incision) on it.

And remove the stone until it is below your finger. And gently push it (the stone) little by little towards the bladder neck.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outside the area where you made the incision.
And tell the assistant to press on the bladder with their hand; and tell another assistant to, with their right hand, pull the genitals upwards, and with their left hand, hold the groin area, the part below the genitals, near the place where the incision is made.
And then insert the spatula properly, which is shaped like this: And cut through the area between the buttocks and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be made on that stone, and your finger be in the buttocks, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the incision outside is wide and inside narrow according to the amount that the stone protrudes, as possible, and not more, for if you press too hard with your finger in the buttocks on the stone near the incision, it will be forcefully expelled without difficulty.
And know that there is some stone with angles and edges, which makes its expulsion difficult because of that.
And that one, which is like a nut, round, is easily expelled.
Therefore, if it is the one with angles and edges, adjust it into the fissure.
And if it is not expelled this way, then you must carefully manipulate it, or gently press it with a tool, the end of which is like a file that you press against the stone, and do not remove it from the area; or gently insert a curved, slender instrument underneath it.
But if you cannot do that, then enlarge the opening slightly, and if you see blood flow, staunch it by pressing with powdered vitriol.
But if the stones are more than one, then at the beginning, you press your hand against the opening of the bladder, and then cut into it; and then gently press further into it; and do the same if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is foolish to cut it with a large incision, because you cause the patient one of two things: either death or the constant leakage of urine, because the area is not completely healed.
But administer an expulsion process until it is expelled, or manipulate its fracture with forceps until you break it into pieces.
Therefore, once you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with aloe juice and dragon's blood, and press it down. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in warmed oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be soothed.
And then lay the patient on their back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
And when you have loosened that, bathe the area with water and warm oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound a hot, burning, and corrosive abscess appears, and it resembles the blood in the bladder being congealed, and because of it, urination is obstructed (and this is known by the blood coming out with the urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it will cause corruption of the bladder and eventually putrefaction.
And then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with whatever else is appropriate for its treatment, until it is healed.
And during all the treatment, you must bind both thighs and adjust the medicines that have been placed on the area.

with a triangular tip, sharp, fitted into a shaft.

And then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, for that is fractured, and it has been forced out with the urine, and the patient is healed.

And a bandage below the stone is not necessary, except to prevent it from returning to the bladder; and the other bandage above it is only necessary to prevent the expulsion of the stone, because when the bandage below the stone is loosened, the stone returns to its place and heals the wound.
And therefore, when you tie the bandage above, it is necessary for you to lift the stone upwards, or it will return towards your hand, and thus heal the wound, as we have said.

Chapter 62.
Of incision on a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing more than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which is beneath the groin's sheath containing the testicle, and is called the scrotum.
And sometimes it is in the proper pouch that nature prepared in the region of the testicle, until it is cared for as there is another testicle; and it is the groin's sheath between the white scrotum that we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a delay.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness that affects the testicles, so that this matter is released into them, and sometimes it occurs due to a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours, for its colour is either leaning towards citron or bloody, or red, or black and slimy, or watery and white, and that is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this collection of water is are these: if it is in the white scrotum we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, sloping slightly lengthwise like an egg shape, and the testicle is visible, because the moisture surrounds that on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper pouch, then the swelling is round with a part resembling a testicle, and thus one mistakenly believes it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the folds of the testicles and the white scrotum, then it lies lower on the groin.
And if you want to know the colour of the moisture, then you probe the colour of the moisture in the swelling with a thin, pointed instrument, whose shape precedes it.  And according to what appears on the trace of the instrument, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to treating it with iron, it is necessary to order the patient to be bled, if possible, and to empty the patient's body.
And afterward, place him on his back on a slightly elevated surface, and put several cloths underneath him.
Then sit on his left side, and command the attendant to sit on his right, who should stretch his staff to one of two places: at the fold of the testicles and at the groin/lower abdomen area.
And then, take a sharp blade and divide the groin crease along its length, roughly towards the pubic area.
And the incision should be made along a straight line, equidistant from the line you should follow on the skin of the groin, until it reaches the visible white tissue.
And scrape away that [tissue], observe, and be careful not to cut into it; and your scraping, with which you scrape it away, should be done gently and subtly, as precisely as possible.
And then pierce the water-filled sac with a wide opening, and extract all the water.
And then, separate the two edges of the incision with forceps, and extend the sac outwards;  do not touch the groin crease, and cut the sac in whatever way is possible for you. And cut it [the sac] entirely or in pieces, and especially its subtle layers, for if you do not carefully examine the incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
However, if the sac protrudes from its cavity during your operation, then once you have completed the sac incision, return it inside, and then rejoin the edges of the groin crease with stitches, and then treat it just like other wounds until it heals.
And if you find the sac corrupted by another illness, then it is necessary that you tie off the vessels that support it, remove the sac, and then treat it as we have described.
But if the water is collected in two distinct places, for example, know that there are two hernias; therefore, cut that part as you did the other one; and if it seems to you to be one operation, then do it.
And then you clean the blood, and apply wool soaked in oil or clarified oil to the incisions; and put another wool soaked in wine and oil over that, and spread that over the groin and the abdominal wound; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bandage it together in this manner:
Then take two cloths, and fill them with wool prepared in this way; and make the ends with which they are pressed with cotton or damp wool, and attach them with a tight band to the undergarments, one end goes from the buttocks to the groin, and the second goes to the area of the thigh, and two ends over the thighs below, and two other ends over the knees, and all the bandages should be fastened to the undergarments.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with a cautery instead of cutting with iron; and you should take a sharp, small knife cautery and with it cut the skin of the groin, and cauterize as we have described until the white membrane containing the water is exposed, and then you take a cautery in this shape: and it is shaped like a Greek letter.
And then pierce through that membrane, and cauterize until all the moisture is expelled.
Then pull the membrane open with forceps, and scrape off the membrane with a heated, sharp cautery; and cut it as much as you can until you eliminate it all; and be careful that the heat doesn't burn the egg sac (testicles/ovary).
And know that this operation is safer and less likely to cause bleeding, and better than an operation with cutting.
However, if you encounter an abscess during the incision or cauterization, or bleeding, then you must treat any complications that arise from this, with a treatment that is appropriate to the situation.
But if the patient is weak and cannot tolerate a second operation due to its severity, then perform the perforation as I have described; and it is when, based on what we have said about the signs, you are certain that the hernia is acute.
Then you should have the patient sit on a raised surface, then pierce the abscess in the lowest part of it with a narrow, flat instrument, and direct the perforation along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the fluid drains out.
And then place a cloth over the area, bind it, and leave it, so that the remaining fluid drains out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the fluid draining, then you pierce it: and this is done because a white film in the opening of the wound creates resistance and prevents the fluid from escaping.  In that case, you should insert a reed or instrument used to draw water from hydrops, the form of which is previously described, or apply gentle pressure to the wound.
And know that the fluid returns, and is retained for approximately six months or thereabouts; and when it is retained, then it should be pierced again in the same way, so that the patient will pass through their days.
Chapter 63.
Of the incision on a fleshy hernia and its treatment.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a cut that extends on the belly from the groin area of the abdominal region, causing the intestines to emerge from that rupture into some of the intestines.
And this rupture is either from the groin or from a straining of it, and these two types occur for many reasons: either by a blow, or by a jump, or by shouting, or by lifting something heavy, or by similar actions.

And the sign of it when it is due to a cut in the groin, is that at the beginning it causes sudden and intense pain; and there is a diverse swelling appearing below the groin area close to the surface, and this is because of the exit of the intestine, and the body of it is in parts outside the groin.
And sometimes the intestine and the membrane are expelled, and this is called an intestinal hernia, or a visceral hernia.
And sometimes it is caused by wind, and sometimes a tear in the intestine occurs, and it is trapped there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it creates severe pain, especially when pressed.

And then take the reed and place it on the prominent area of the groin, and press it to the area of the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent areas above the groin's reed-like (elongated) area, and join one of those to the other with stitching.
And then pull out the reed, and do not cut the groin completely, nor extend the testicle, nor anything else, as I have instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment for ulcers, and as you would cut away pus, cleanse it and restore the ulcer until it is healed; for the groin is contracted and the rupture's prominence is not aligned.
And cautery is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of softening the tightness of the testicles.
Often the tightness of the testicles in some men is softened by this, because the flesh between them is softened, and care for them is required.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one attending to the cure to have the patient lie according to their side, and to spread out all the softened tissue that surrounds the natural tightness; and then to bring the two edges together with stitches.
And if you wish, stitch the excess of softened tissue at the beginning with three or four stitches, and with firm stitching.
And then, you should make incisions between the stitches; and then treat with a cure for other wounds until it is healed and the pus subsides.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our law, is forbidden, but for a reason, it is permitted to me to mention it in this book.  Its mention is necessary for two reasons: one, so that the physician, when asked about it, will know how to treat the condition it causes; and the second reason is that we often need to castrate other animals for our own benefit, such as oxen, goats, pigs, and animals similar to these.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by a cut to sever.
However, in the method of castration by crushing, the procedure is to place the animal in hot water until its genitals soften and hang loose. And then grasp them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by cutting and severing, you must hold the animal and press the scrotum with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut into each testicle with a cut until both testicles are severed.
And then cut those [parts] further, once each is scraped/skinned. And do not leave behind any cause from the scrotum, except the subtle membrane that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration, which is done by scraping, is better, because vigorous scraping leaves no cause in the testicles for the animal to desire mating.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when it appears as a space in the scrotum, between the testicles, in the shape of a woman's vulva, containing hair.
And sometimes urine flows from the area in the scrotum between the testicles.
But in women, it is one type: and it is that the vulva of the woman, on the pubic area, is like two testicles of a man, slightly enlarged outwardly, in the same way as a man's foreskin and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: for two of the men and one type for women. And it is necessary that you incise those adhering flesh until the pressure subsides, and then treat them with the remedies for other wounds.
And in the second type of men, concerning urine in the scrotum, there is no cure nor operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and protruding flesh due to women's conditions.
A mild swelling is often caused by natural factors until it subsides and the condition improves; and sometimes it becomes severe in some women to the point of spreading as in men and extending to the area of intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess swelling with your hand or with a scalpel.
And do not over-cut, especially in the area of the root, lest you cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with remedies for sores until it heals.
However, a fleshy growth that arises within the mother's womb until it fills it, and easily comes out at the openings in the form of a tail (and therefore some early doctors would call this "a tail-like disease"); thus, it must be cut as one cuts swelling, and treated until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of dropsy.
Dropsy is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental dropsy is due to a preceding illness, and this is caused either by flesh that is not properly joined or by a subtle or thick fluid.
And it occurs either in the region of the mother's womb, or in her ribs, or in her upper or lower abdomen, and you forbid cooking, consumption, and childbirth, and strongly forbid menstruation.
And the diagnosis is made by observing the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent, promptly; but if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your words or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a subtle fluid, and is close to the two lips, then you should cook and break it apart.
And you should place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers on both lips together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcefully spread the two lips until that subtle fluid is broken and the obstruction is open.
Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the affected area, and let the patient rest there for the day so that the incision does not become consolidated.
But if the fluid is thick and dense, then you must cut it with a sharp instrument (perhaps a lancet) or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to a clot, then lift that clot with forceps, and cut it open.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing bites, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and linseed, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it in place for a few days and apply warm compresses.
And then treat it with another healing treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes, it affects the surrounding tissues, and then it needs to be incised using this method. But if it is due to a boil, a boil located in the head should not be pierced with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of a red swelling that appears in the female's vagina.
Hemorrhoids are the swelling of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and if the hemorrhoids are long-standing and ulcers have formed.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of those [hemorrhoids] and their signs, and I will now say which ones receive treatment and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and ulcers, when they are deep within the anus and do not appear on the surface, no treatment with iron should be applied.
But those which are on the opening of the anus, where one can feel them, are the ones that can be treated.
And you should place the woman in a cool place and then carefully cut the ulcer with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and remove the roots.
And then sprinkle over them the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without biting, such as acacia and similar ones.
And then soak their feet for an hour, and then place them in cool water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make the patient sit in a decoction of sumat (likely a plant), and the bark of pomegranates, walnuts, and similar things.
And then take barley flour, mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a poultice with it and place it on their back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without incision.
And then take wool, soak it in the juice of coriander or the juice of arnagloza (possibly a similar plant) or the juice of germander mixed with diluted wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the accidental perforation of the breast (or chest).
Many types of abscesses can occur in the breast, such as cancer, stony abscesses, ulcers, boils, creeping sores, fistulas, hemorrhoids, fistulas, and other abscesses. And I have already described these illnesses and their types, their symptoms, and their treatments in the division.
And I must discuss in this treatise the acute abscess that occurs in the breast when it is one of the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know how the operation with an iron is performed.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing, phlegm, and fever, and whether there is redness and if you feel it when pressed, then do not rush with puncturing it, but treat it with what assists in maturation until the severity of the condition subsides.
Then you must have the woman lie on a bed with two supports; have her recline on her side and place her feet at the lowest part of the abdomen, and her thighs spread out; put her arms under her temples; and bind each limb with suitable bandages.
And then the midwife, standing on the right side, administers the instrument with which the opening in the breast is made, the shape of which will be evident in the procedures that will follow.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you must insert, with the rod, the nipple of the woman's breast, and what enters the breast from the instrument should be no more than the nipple and the surrounding area. But if the instrument is larger than the nipple, then you must place powders on the nipple's surface, so that the instrument is prevented from entering the nipple.
And you must place the liquid that is on the instrument's tip on the upper side, and have the attendant hold the instrument and turn the liquid until the opening of the breast is exposed.
And when the secretion appears, and it is touched with the hand and is slippery and moist, then you must dry that around the head with a cloth.
And when the discharge is evacuated, then you must place a piece of cloth soaked in warm oil or green oil (in which some astringent is present) on the area; and place the cloth on the nipple area.
And place outside the nipple, and over the chest, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And it should be treated for approximately two days with the ointment that is appropriate for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the nipple and the area are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long gentian has been boiled, and then return to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep within the nipple, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron, instead treating it with what we have mentioned in the division.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when they develop in a non-natural way.
It is necessary for the midwife to understand the natural birthing process from its initial signs.  When you see the woman pushing downwards and desiring to breathe the air, and is relieved by the weight pressing on her during labor, and hastens the delivery of the embryo, then know that this birth is natural.  The delivery should be of the embryo at its head, and the afterbirth either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, the abdomen should be compressed so that the embryo descends easily.  For when it descends by its head, it also releases the afterbirth, and it is cleansed of excess material with good preparation.
However, a birth that is different from this is unnatural and undesirable. For when the embryo is delivered on its feet, and its body emerges before its head and feet, and one hand, or one foot, or the head emerges, or the belly and is turned forcefully onto its side, and its form is not acceptable.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against errors and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of those things, and what the meaning is within them, so that it may be understood and be established upon them.
When the birth is expelled by natural expulsion, and the woman is struggling with it, and it is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then apply compresses, or what women use, and vaporize the head with collected fenugreek and moist oils.
And then the midwife should gently insert her fingers into the small space and tear the afterbirth, or cut it with her fingernail until she removes the moisture within it. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman must be treated with a poultice of ptilium and a poultice of fenugreek with cumin.
Then command her, have her be treated, to be squeezed and make her sneeze with incantations; and cover her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled soon.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at first, then it is necessary that you gently and gradually return them. But if you do not succeed in pushing it back in, then place the woman on a board and lift her legs up, and then gently move the board down to the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the movement.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then cut them and remove the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to the woman's hand with a cloth and remove it carefully, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push it upwards, and then turn the fetus gently, and then wrap it, and then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
But when it has emerged into the thighs, then you command him to strain, and make him sneeze with condiments, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you gently return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with a light expulsion.
And if it is not expelled by all of that which we have recounted, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, and crush them well in a mortar, and then apply this to the area around the woman's vagina, at the lower part of her abdomen. And then apply this warm water compress, until the hypochondria is relieved.
And when the parts around him are soothed, then give him a fumigation of myrtle and place that close to him. And after the fumigation for an hour, then you place him on a bed, and make him sneeze, and hold his mouth shut, and lift from that, and gently compress the lower part of his abdomen, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus emerges lengthwise, hanging by one of its hands. If it is possible to reduce the hand of it (the fetus), then do so. But if that cannot be reduced, then lift the woman and have her walk.
And if walking is not possible, then have her lie down on the bed, and forcefully massage the thighs, and then administer bandages made of mucilages.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, and then focus on reducing the hand, and reposition the fetus according to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
The fetus emerges with its arms outstretched, and its face is towards the mother's back. The midwife should hold the baby's hand and gently turn it, and move it with ease.
And when it is repositioned, then place on its head what we have mentioned of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to move slowly, and when you have done that, then have her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean to the right side, and then instruct her to strain; and make her sneeze with remedies. The preparation of the fetus's exit onto the side.
When the mother is delivered of the afterbirth in this manner, consider, and then you cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended by its face and neck, then you cut it at the navel, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into its parts until you have placed it as is necessary, and tell the woman to strain herself several times, and it will be expelled quickly if God wills it.
The delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born frequently, and sometimes three or four, but this is unusual and rarely happens; however, some things are outside of nature, and not alive.
And count those, whether they have multiplied, a second time, and separate them by a piece; distinguish them at the ligament in the navel of each of them.  And sometimes they are expelled as easily as one fetus through natural means, and sometimes through unnatural means as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult during their delivery and reach the point that we have described of unnatural figures, then you treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious in every way according to how the patient is prepared for healing.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  I have verified that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all fully formed.  This was determined through the measurements of renowned scholars.
And all this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for it is easily understood from that small resemblance, and therefore one must proceed with what is appropriate.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with what we have discussed in the division of medicine and it hasn't emerged from the body, and you are forced to use manual procedures, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and not suffering from any of the conditions that could endanger her life.
Then the woman should be placed on her back on the bed, and her head should be lowered and her legs raised.
And the attendant should hold her (the woman) in two places, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body does not move away during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with moist oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingers into the fetus.
And suitable places for this: then consider if the fetus is prepared to descend headfirst, and then hook your fingers into its forehead, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or in its armpits, or near the ribs, or under the lower ribs.
But if the descent is feet first, then in the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that the finger(s) be held with the right hand, and place its curve in the area between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and place the fingers into any of those places we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty space/opening.
And then place another finger, or a third if necessary, in contact with it, so that there is a drawing together, and not just a stretching out according to its natural shape alone.
And be moved in all directions, so that its extraction is eased, just as is done with a tooth during its removal.
And it is necessary for the area between them that the stretching be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert those fingers and turn with them whatever is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fet (perhaps a piece of tissue) is expelled, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until all of the fet is expelled.
However, if the hand has emerged before the other, and it is not possible to return it by compressing it, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is completely expelled, and cut it either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary that you do the same for the other hand and for both feet.
But if the head of the fet is large, or if compression causes it to swell, or if water is collected at its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the bony protuberances, to split the head and remove the water, or to cut it with an instrument called an "alundat" (a type of surgical instrument), the form of which will be shown with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter immediately following this one.
And similarly do if the fet is naturally large-headed; and then it is necessary to cut it with a scalpel, or to remove it with an alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has emerged and is compressed towards the crotch, then it should be split until the moisture within the feet is expelled.
But if it is not complicated, nor subject to swelling, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the belly is suppurated or hydrops, then it is necessary that you pierce it until the moisture within it drains out.
But if the fet is on its feet, then the traction of it is gentle and its direction towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is compressed towards the belly and the feet, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and split the belly and feet until that which is within them drains out.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she inserts her hand into the air cavity, and searches for the head with it, and pulls it with the said fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then inserts a finger, or two fingers, into it, and pulls it with them.
And if the mother's mouth is closed by a festering abscess that has afflicted it, then it is necessary that it not be forced; beforehand, it is necessary to administer a runoff of oily, humid things. And administer plasters; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetuses is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned: and what we have said about the living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no cause inside the afterbirth.
And when you have completed all your procedure, then administer what we have said about the treatment of the abscesses that occur from the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then immerse in astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a fetus was dead, and soon after that fire, again pregnant, and soon after a dead fetus again. A long time ago, an abscess occurred near his navel; and it swelled until it burst, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, and the wound was not healed.
Then I applied some strong-drawing plaster to him, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones in the abdomen.
And then I thought they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I treated the wound, and removed many bones from it, the woman being in her best condition, and lived in that way for a long time, recovering from the little amount of pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather that it is science and helpful to what the skilled physician provides with their hands for treatment.
Of the forms of the instruments necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of dizziness with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of constriction in which the books are bound, with which binding are two twists at the two ends of two strings. However, this twist should be more subtle than a constricting band, and it should be made either of thread or of a wooden stick.
And the width of each stick is about two fingers, and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle place of the two sticks are two attachments of that same stick, which are already fastened, the length of which is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two attachments are inserted into the mother's mouth, so that it is opened when you revolve the two prescribed twists.
Another form similar to that one, more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of wood or a stick, following the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity, there are two attachments, just as before, and the length of those attachments is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with these, then have the woman lie on the bed, feet hanging down, opening the space between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument like you do with shears, as much as you want to open the mother's mouth until the woman does what you want.

Of extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with remedies, and firmly press your hand over her mouth and nostrils.  If by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot, and pierce a hole in its cover, and place inside herbs, opening the mother's mouth, like camomile, rue, anise, chamomile, arabic cicadas, cassia wood, and St. John's wort. All of these herbs, or some of them, are submerged in water in the pot, and place that on the fire.
