And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
However, if this treatment remains ineffective and the afterbirth has not been expelled, then you instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in mallow infusion, and then insert it into the vagina and seek the afterbirth with it.  And when she has found it, then take it and extend it little by little. But if it is attached to the mother's womb, then she should insert her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, she should extract it slowly, as directed, so that the mother does not suffer undue stress. Beforehand, it is necessary that it be moved from side to side with ease.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with herbal remedies.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself out by repeatedly trying to remove it. For this will cause it to rot over a few days, dissolve, and be expelled.
But when it rots, a bad odor rises from the head and stomach, and this torments the patient.  Therefore, soothing fumes and appropriate remedies must be administered.
However, some of the ancients already tested the use of fumigation with ground-up resin and dried figs.
Method of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for retained menstrual blood and afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or clay.
And place the narrow end in the receptacle and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigating substance should be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let the person holding it ensure that the fumigating substance is consumed and then replace it with more.
Concerning the treatment of an unperforated child's anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which are then described as narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that constriction with her finger; and if not, to perforate it with a sharp instrument, taking care not to touch the sphincter.
And then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear it is obstructed, then insert a cannula into the opening for several days, and remove it when the child is ready to swallow.
And anal obstruction can occur due to the consolidation of a plague or an abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the treatment and lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that arise from below.
Fistulas that originate in the lower area,  being swollen and thick, which arise near the anus externally or in the space of the lower region.
And it is a fistula or several.
And when that swelling is old and flows with moist, white moisture or a subtle putridity, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those that are penetrating are known by what emerges from them through discharge and by windiness; and sometimes the patient's discharge and a strong outflow of worms are present from them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder and into the urethra; and some of these penetrating ones reach the hip joint and the groin.
And how a penetrating fistula into the rectum is distinguished from a non-penetrating one is that you should insert your index finger into the rectum and gently introduce a thin probe of iron or of some other material into the fistula if there is no twisting or obstruction.
But if there is twisting in it, then introduce a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel any of this at all, nor does any discharge, worms, or windiness proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, then the sign of it is leakage of urine from it and prohibition of urination until the area is consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and causes pain around it, then the sign of it is that the probe passes into it if there is no twisting into the bone, and causes pain around the area; and that it may be permanent; and that no medicine will work on it, nor can it be consolidated with ointments.
And for all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is the work and vain effort of foolish physicians, upon which you should not rely.
But in the case of one that is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected, as I will tell you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies with their hands under their stomach, and their legs raised up, their thighs bent towards their stomach, and then insert a lead probe, or an iron one if the fistula is twisted, until you know the spot where the probe passes through.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe penetrating through your finger, or something like flesh or pus between your finger and the fistula, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, because there is no cure for it, as we have said.
But perhaps someone might say that it is cured in some men slowly.
And the treatment through which improvement is expected is that you apply a fine heated cautery, according to the width of the fistula, as described, and insert this heated object into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the flesh that is fused together in the fistula-like a bird feather's quill - has been cauterized.
And then treat it with some bandages until all the fused flesh has healed, and then treat it with medicines that encourage the growth and healing of flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it will not be cured at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate completely into your finger that is in the anus, and between it and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or pus, and the fistula extends to the surface of the skin, then cauterize the skin at the beginning of the fistula, and use the probe to cauterize, and keep it in the fistula until the cauterization reaches the place where the probe's tip ends, and then the probe is removed and discarded.
And then cleanse those fistulaed tissues, which are like the hollow quill of a bird's feather, leaving no cause for concern.
But if bleeding occurs between you and your procedure while cauterizing those tissues, the best and easiest tool is to cauterize with fire or an acute medicine, for cauterization with fire has two good effects: it cauterizes the fused tissues, and it stops the blood flow and removes excess moisture.
And then treat it with bandages or a woven cloth until the wound is well-covered, and then treat the wound with ointments that promote the growth and healing of hard flesh.
And those medicines that contain astringent and drying properties; when this is done, the wound is already healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula extends to the anus and is distant from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And therefore, if you feel a resistance and between you and it is a tough membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation to be done there, but rather to proceed with a route of trust and hope.
This condition can be treated in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it until you reach its cavity, so you can insert remedies and promote healing; and do not proceed too forcefully with cutting, for fear of injuring the surrounding tissues and causing the patient unnecessary pain or injury; and then treat it with what we have previously described, for a quick healing will result; or separate the membrane and cleanse the pus that collects within, near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp end, and then clean the fistula wounds within the cut, where you can apply a scab; and then strive to restore the whole wound through cutting, and if the opening above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the anal area, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this manner: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus near the body surface, with the string or at the edge of the anus, then use a probe with a pointed end, which is similar to a needle prepared in this way:
and insert it into that, wrapped with five threads or around them; and then insert the probe with a thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity.
However, if it penetrates the edge of the anus close to the inside, then you pull the thread from that hole as if you were inserting your finger into the anus; and pull out the thread's end and tie the two ends together, tightening each one, and leave it for a day or two. And whenever you cut the thread into the flesh, firmly tighten it until the flesh between the two ends of the thread is cut and falls off.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way you are prepared, but not too deeply, because it is not up to you to do so lest you cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the spatula with which the fistula is cut: one curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that you don't cut what's unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks like bunches of grapes, or they are like the grapes of a bunch. And they are small and large, and the blood flows from them constantly and is one, or many, and they are outside the anus, and on its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the degree of little moisture, from which runs yellow water or a little blood with a continuous flow, and it is according to the color of the body.
And the cure for those that are inside the anus is to command the patient to sit down and to strain until the anus is open and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp and lift them with tweezers or your nails, and then cut them away from their roots.
But if the tweezers cannot hold them because of their moisture and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool cloth, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle some hot powders over them near the incision, to act as a cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then prick the patient with a small-pointed instrument, and wash it with what is inside the anus, and make it open slightly when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause is superficial, and you should grasp them with your finger or tweezers, and cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And when you have made the incision, you must apply sutures to them in this manner: take a thread wrapped around it, insert it into a needle, and then pull the hemorrhoids apart, and pierce it with the needle on the other side, and tie the two ends of the thread on the part below the needle, laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong tightening, then tie the thread and remove the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one unsutured, so that excess blood can drain from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in warm oil or ointment on the anus; and place it on the anus and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it subsides; and when it has subsided, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And since they are old and no longer respond to that treatment, then you should scrape them with the point of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the skin pus on top is removed, which prevents them from healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you should scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first, until much blood and they are raw; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a boil, that is a key point, and is a small round sore in the color of the skin, resembling a nailhead, and arises on the whole body, most often in the parts below the feet and on the fingers, and usually results in pain due to its presence.
And then it is necessary to incise what is around the boil, and hold it with forceps or tweezers, and uproot it from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described in the preceding chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round prominence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And the treatment for it is to scrape and cut that, or to squeeze it with a file or a piece of wood until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize this, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
However, a pimple is a small, fleshy or fistulous, large prominence on the body's surface, often found on the buttocks.  When the cold encounters it, the affected person feels a sensation similar to an ant bite, and it appears around the body.
And the treatment for it is to take a hollow tube, made of a hawk's or a vulture's feather, which is strong.  Place that over the pimple until the tube surrounds the pimple completely. Then, move your hand with the tube until you cut that which is in the pimple's circuit, and place it onto the surrounding flesh, to eradicate the pimple from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron according to this method:
The part above the tube should be thin, closed (that is, unperforated), so that the grip on it is sharpened and its movement is smooth.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously described in the section on cauterization of boils in the chapter on cauterization.

Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of plague treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I now say that the treatment of those with hands and medicines follows the completion (or fulfillment).
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on how they are caused and the location where the plagues appear.
And the causes by which plagues are often brought about are like a stone blow, or an incision with a knife or sword, or a blow with a spear, or with a stick, or an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the plagues that occur in the body's locations are like a plague that strikes the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the stomach, or the liver, and similar ones in the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with simple head plagues, for the recollection of compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone blow or similar, and the skull has only been cracked, and it is a large wound, and you fear the development of a hot abscess in the patient, then you should quickly apply leeches and phlebotomize the cephalic vein immediately; and draw blood from the patient according to the strength of the patient, unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that issues from the wound is scant.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the affected area; and guard the patient from repletion and from heavy food and drink.
But if some cause forbids this (phlebotomy), then reduce the patient's food intake and keep them from meat and strong drink.
And if a hot abscess occurs on the wound, apply a poultice of roasted olive oil or wine with astringents.
But if a hot abscess is certain, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and if the air hasn't changed the powder I will soon describe in detail.
And if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or from an incision of a space or similar, and its edges are not brought together by sutures, then bring them together with stitches, as I will describe later on the suturing of wounds.
However, if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and there is no retention of it but only a slight protrusion, then cut it away and subsequently treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh, roughly in the location of the original tissue. Advise the patient to eat foods rich in fat: cabbage, thickened broth, and similar, until it heals.
And if you find putrefaction in the tissue, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied material and discard it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow, and it is not severed by medicines, then you must locate the artery, and if you find it unsevered, then you cut it with a knife, or tie it off; and if necessity dictates that the above methods are not suitable, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore, you should sprinkle this powder over them, for it will consolidate them before the air alters them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts unquenched lime; thoroughly grind and sift them.  Then fill the wound with the mixture and bind it tightly until it adheres well and the blood coagulates.
Then bind it tightly with dressings and leave it, and do not remove them until it is no longer inflamed, for the medicine will not be effective until the wound is healed.
And sometimes this alone is enough, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood available.
And the same procedure works on large wounds when it is necessary to apply it to them and bind them tightly, especially fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air hasn't yet altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially during summer, then this powder application is not appropriate.  Instead, treat them with what we have already described, or apply digestive ointments, or powder made from barley flour mixed with water and honey until it forms a paste; then treat them with the other cures until they are healed.
But if a small fracture occurs in the bone, then you extract it with skill.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds of the neck.
But if a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between that and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerve or artery of the neck is cut, then if it is a nerve, do not apply anything that causes strong astringency, such as zinc or vinegar, which is vitriol, and similar things, for they harm the nerves and counteract healing. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold and its continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the limbs.
And if this is treated with mild medicines, such as washing with clean water, sometimes infused into oiled or un-oiled ointment, and poultices when washed with clean water and other soft stones following this method, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or close its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or has descended to the lower part of the flesh, then pierce it in the most depressed location.
But if an artery has been incised in the wound and blood flow has resulted, then separate or cut it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the entire neck have been incised, and the veins of the neck are saved, then you should stitch the edges of the wound along the neck bone and do not touch it; first align it and return it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with suitable bandages and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments that have a styptic and healing effect until it is healed.
But if the wound has reached the bone, you must treat it, and if there are fragments of bone within it, remove all you can; and what you are unable to remove, leave until the wound begins to fester and separates, and the discharge is reduced.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle the powder recommended to you on it. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with whatever remedy is mentioned in the appropriate chapter.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you note its depth, then consider further if the wound is ventilated when the patient breathes; if so, know that the wound is fatal.
And if there is no great depth to the wound, and it is recent with its blood, then you place on it at first powder, and do not press it so that the blood is not trapped in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put an attracting ointment on it, and remove it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then place old cloth over the wound opening so that it absorbs the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that the trapped fluids drain out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound, and you do not observe spasms, tremors, or a bad heart condition, or constriction of the windpipe, and the other conditions are good, then know that the wound is salvageable; and then treat it with poultices and other remedies until healed.
But if healing is impossible, and the wound is perpetually open, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its own specific procedures.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or blade, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with sutures if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if the wound has penetrated the bone, and already detached pieces are present, then you should try to extract those detached pieces by forcing the wound open. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound has healed, for the extraction of those pieces will be easier then.
And for other wounds occurring on other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as that which we have already described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment which occurs in the abdomen is either severe, or mild, or moderate.
A small rupture is more difficult to repair in another way, and if the reduction of them is more difficult, unless you quickly return the intrusion of them to their place, for it opens and enlarges, making their re-insertion difficult.
Therefore, the best rupture is moderate, because in that one, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that abdominal suturing is performed in four ways, but two sutures are common and applicable to the suturing of other wounds in the body.
And two other specific sutures are applicable to other wounds, but nonetheless, they are more pertinent to abdominal suturing.
And I will explain each to you with exposition and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to reduce, then this is in one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you must warm it as follows: you should submerge a sponge or damp cloth in warm water alone, or in water with squill, cyperus, and pepper, and apply this to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with vinegar, which has astringent properties, and is better than just water in resolving the inflammation.
And once you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow root and mallow have been boiled, for this alleviates the discomfort with little effort.
But if the discomfort is not relieved by this treatment, then carefully cut the rupture a little with an instrument used to treat hemorrhoids.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate and does not involve cutting, and there should be no attempt to dry it out unnecessarily and risk further harm.
Concerning the instrument, note its shape:
One part of it is sharply curved, the other part is not sharp, and the tip is slender, but not as slender as a spatula; rather, it is slightly blunt.
And thus, if the wound is aggravated and the intestine has protruded, then its reduction should be according to its natural form, back into its proper place, if possible; for that is best.
But when the rupture is wide and in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise the patient's head and feet higher than their lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then always keep your attention and consideration on this, so that the part where the wound is is higher than the other.
And thus you should apply this to large wounds and to medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant hold the rupture with his hand and close the edges, and then determine whether there is any other reason for the suture or additional cause.
And this is the common method of suture: one who takes the needle, or a count of needles according to the size of the wound; and then places the tip of the rupture on the ring finger, and tightens the needle one, or another, so that you don't put faith in the two ends of the cord, with two ends of the thread that is under the cord of the inner parts, until you have made it penetrate that part; and you have already joined the two ends of the cord and the two ends of the thread, making four knots.
And then tighten with a double thread around the needle, and sometimes from two parts together, and then close the two edges and the wound with a proper closure; and then leaves the thickness of the finger and puts another needle into that, and then ties that with thread just as it was tied with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness between thick and thin, for thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the thread, and therefore, they should be medium in both thinness and thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted into both ends of the thread too quickly, so that the flesh is not pierced easily and the wound is broken open before its healing.
And do not prolong the suture so that the wound is prevented from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient during their rest; and you will place on the suture, powders spread everywhere, that hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know the wound has healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, for one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient for their suture.
However, the second suture is common:  and it is that you suture the four ends of the thread and two ends of the cloth simultaneously, with a needle that is proportionally thin and thick enough.
And then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, withdraw the needle from the same place where you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part to the wound, so that the suture is like the stitching used to join fabrics together.
And place between each suture the thickness of a little finger.
And this suture is known/recognised by men.
And with such a suture, I closed the wound that had befallen a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife.  The wound was a gap/break of more than a hand's breadth, and the intestines had protruded about two hand-breadths from the middle of the wound.  The rupture was in the middle of the member; and thus I reduced that protrusion of the intestines, which had been outside the wound for 24 hours.

But I shall describe two kinds of sutures, proper according to the account of Scripture.

And then, when you have made that penetration, you then make that penetration again in the same wound end from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and push the needle into the other end of the wound from the inside to the outside.
And push with that your penetration with which you push the penetration into the thread at the wound end, which is on the patient's side, until all of that penetrates.

And do this once and again, and do this once more again until you have sewn all that is outside following that example.
And the summary of this method of suturing is that you stitch with a hide-like stitch, with which you sew skin, so that you stitch the thread once from the thread end, and you leave it again until you have completed it.

Of the treatment of sores and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and is ulcerated and not healed and continuously discharges pus and is not excised, then, in any limb, is universally called a fistula and we call that a sore.
And a fistula, according to truth, is a hard, white, fibrous growth, without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's nest; and for this reason, that ulcer is called a "wrenched ulcer," that is, a nest.
And in some people it is moist, which discharges pus; and easily the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes large, and sometimes small.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their cure with medicines, in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you aware of the treatment of fistula, which is done through surgery with the hand.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for among those are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the spinal vertebrae, or to any of the joints formed by such composition as of the hand and foot; and those are those that damage the proximity of the principal limb and similar things.
And therefore, that fistula among these, which is in this way, is among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, and does not receive cure, and dealing with it is labor and ignorance.
And if any of these fistulas does not go to the depths of those places mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and want to understand it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula runs in a straight line, explore it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, because of the lightness of its body, is light and bends towards the tortuosity.
And if the fistula is very grievous, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe.
Then irrigate the opening through its openings, for the moisture with which you irrigate it goes towards the other openings and flows from them.
And then inquire meticulously, according to the manner that is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the cavity of the fistula is distant or close, or if it is a fistula with many openings and this all lies within the totality of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the location of the pain through your pressure with your hand on the area, and similar indications and the accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all those matters with true discernment, then proceed to the cure according to confidence; and it is that you now consider whether the fistula is a superficial, nearby fistula, or in a salty location, distant from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places that I have mentioned to you.
Then you dry the fistula according to what has previously been said about its description, and remove what is in that filtration and corrupt flesh, and that which has arisen in the opening of the flesh, or of pus and similar things, and cure that until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of deep location, and it is considered appropriate, then it is necessary to cut into it as far as possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh; and then administer some compresses with sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have opened with an iron tool.
And do this with those compresses several times until the sharp medicine corrodes everything that remains in the cavity of the fistula of the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you must inquire into it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not find any cause of a vein or nerve or major limb that prohibits it, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape that until all corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that causes flesh to grow until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and still emits pus as before, then know that you have not achieved complete eradication of the corruption; and therefore you must expose it again carefully, thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your final strength, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a distant depth of the body, which has not been reached, and therefore there is no remedy for you but to leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then you remove it with delicate forceps suitable for that.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously perform the extraction of all of them, and leave no trace of them with your strength.
However, if you encounter any difficulty due to moisture, and in their incision there is no fear or error, then cut those ligaments or flesh if they are present, and expose the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then place something over the wound that may close the wound, and leave it until the area around those bones is free of flesh and ligaments, and be careful that the wound does not become consolidated, and that the incision you made in your procedure is constricted.
However, if you fear it, place on it cotton soaked in oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian unguent, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments will be softened, and the bones are separated from them, the removal, and the extraction of them, is eased.
But if it is a large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, or similar to those, and only the surface of it is corrupted, then scrape that with a rasp only on the back until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the part of it that is corrupted is a large portion, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and cut all the way to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I will recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use it as an example and help for your procedure.
There was a young man, around 30 years of age, who experienced pain in his thigh.  The swelling/matter within the body spread until it reached the thigh.  It was a matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and it was not caused by an external factor.
And then the time passed with this condition due to the error of the physicians until the abscess opened, and many matters flowed out.  His recovery was delayed until the leg became fistulous, and many openings formed, which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
And so, many physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were truly skilled in the art, until I was called in.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings throughout his body; and the man's entire body was emaciated and his complexion was yellow.
Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings and the probe reached the bone.
Then I carefully examined all the openings and found them leading to various parts of the leg.
Then I incised one of those openings until I exposed part of the bone, and found it decayed, already black and rotten; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, having penetrated deep into the marrow.
Then I closed what I had exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it was not in the bone itself, but rather that corruption that I had cut out and closed, and I had already cauterized it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I again opened and exposed the bone a second time, over the first exposure, and found continued decay with the bone.
And I closed what appeared to me of that decay, and then I was forced to attempt the healing and restoration of it, but it has not been restored.
And then I exposed it again, and did not cease to cut into the bone little by little, in particular, and attempted its restoration, but it was not restored until I cut around a palm's width of it, and removed its marrow.  Then I restored it with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And further operations or cutting into it are not necessary, except for the weakness of the patient, their low tolerance, and their fear of death, for he was experiencing fainting spells at all times due to excessive evacuations/drainage.
And thus healed is by complete healing, and there is nothing in place of bone but hard flesh; and its condition in his body is good, and its strength returned; and it is restored to its proper condition, and I did not see any trouble in his walking or harm in any way.
But if the bone is protruding from the body, then you must cut it in this way: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone, and instruct someone to pull it outward; then place a thicker wool bandage over the first bandage; and now tie it over the flesh below the bone, pulling the end to draw the flesh back into place, and you should expose the flesh where you want to cut, so the cut does not damage the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or plank under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut will not be prevented from cutting through the corrupt flesh.
And the cutting should be done little by little over the affected area, because if the corruption is within the cavity of the bone and not apparent on its surface, more cutting will be needed.
And if the bone is corrupted but not protruding, with one part still attached to the other, and the corruption is in the middle or a part of it, then you should expose all the flesh; then put wood underneath, and then cut from the first part where the corruption is until the cut extends to the other part.
And let the cutting be done according to the location of the corruption, a little at a time, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is connected to the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not in a logical place but in a hidden, indirect one.
And if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever manner it appears to you, and expose it, and clean it according to every possible arrangement, and by any means, to be corrected, as long as it does not conflict with any vein or nerve.
And know that the instruments for cutting and sawing these bones are very specific to the placement of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, their size, their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore, you must prepare a suitable instrument for each type of operation.
And know that this operation indicates to you the type of instrument you need; when great study, long experience, and knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience with illnesses, are yours, then you yourself will know what instruments are appropriate for every illness.

Of incision tips and the sawing of bones.
The extremities [of the affected area] may be affected either by an internal or external cause, or if your treatment with those medicines fails to resolve the corruption.  Or, sometimes, the corruption moves within the limb, unchecked by any external cause.  Then it is necessary that you incise that limb where the corruption is most present, to prevent the patient from succumbing to death or the worsening of the limb's condition.
And the sign of the affected area is that the limb turns black, as if fire has burned it, or it gradually darkens until the hardness spreads throughout that limb, seizing it and affecting the whole body; and therefore, treat it with incisions.
And similarly, if the corruption is caused by the effects of poisonous creatures such as scorpions, vipers, or other similar things.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you incise the finger, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you incise it at the root of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the upper arm.
And if, instead, it affects the arm, then you incise the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond the elbow, then in that case death is the fate of the patient; and administering any other treatment for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do for the foot; when corruption reaches the toe of the foot, then you incise it down to one of the bones.
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you incise the entire foot. But if it rises to the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no cure for them, but only release, and leaving the patient to death.
And the way to incise or cut the limb is to tighten the ligament in the part just below where you want to incise, and tighten another ligament over the spot.  And the minister should extend a ligament downwards, and the minister extend another ligament upwards; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a sharp tool until all the flesh is exposed; and then it is to be incised or cut.
And it is necessary to place cloths on all sides so that the area does not come into contact with the body, and that the patient receives a well-fitted and carefully treated wound.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while working, then you cauterize the wounded area carefully, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man on his foot, the same accident that I and you have recounted, which is that he suffered a dark discoloration on his foot, with a burn similar to a fire's combustion.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it spread to his entire foot.
And then that man, realizing that the treatment was going to the affected limb with the intensity of the pain and burn he felt, himself cut open that foot down to the joint, and the wound healed.
For a long time, that same corruption happened to him in the finger of his index hand.
And then he came to me, and I was compelled to counteract that excess with what I could place on his hand, to cleanse his body; but the excess was not repelled, and it began to move to the other fingers until it spread to his entire hand.
And he urged me to cut open his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and fearing the death of his hand due to the incision, because the patient's strength was waning.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and later it came to me that that man prepared himself and cut his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have not related this story except to be of assistance to those who suffer from this illness, and to serve as a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas using medicines.
When an abscess occurs on a fleshy limb, and the space of the abscess extends until it collects pus, and it is then broken and perforated, and the pus is released, and the place remains empty, like a vessel, and the surrounding skin, like thin cloth, has not undergone further corruption, and it has not affected the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and is not called a fistula.
However, if the space extends until the corruption affects any part of the limb, then it is called a fistula, and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to cut away that surrounding skin completely, especially if it is thinned and like cloth, and ensure that it is not adhered to the affected area due to its corruption.
But if you expect the skin to adhere and that corruption has not progressed further, and the skin has broken open, then you treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the arm you are treating has a foul odor, then give an enema with Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, sour wine, and vinegar taken in equal parts, and combined in a vessel, and heated over the fire until the medicine turns reddish-brown and acquires the consistency of honey.
Then, take what is necessary of it and dissolve it with water and honey; and use this mixture as an enema for the sinus, and compress the opening, and leave the medicine in place for two hours.
Then, remove that with a compress; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.
And sometimes it is treated with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and you should take the ash of wood, and put water on it; and then strain that and use it to give an enema until you believe the sinus is closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then give an enema with water and honey mixed with crushed vinegar, and give an enema with wine and honey mixed, because honey has the property of cleansing and washing, and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and believe the sinus is healed, then give an enema with something that promotes the growth of flesh in that place.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with warmed oil and astringent wine; or give an enema with other medicines and ointments that have the properties that I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so the enema tube cannot be inserted, then widen it gradually with a small iron, and apply heat wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the edges together with sutures and leave it at a size that the enema tube can enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is elevated, then drain it to the lowest part, so the pus flows downwards, because when the pus is trapped within the depth of the sinus, flesh will develop there.
And if it is not possible for you to drain the sinus to the desired lowest point, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation through which the pus flows easily, as is appropriate, and that no impediment is left in the sinus to hinder the flow of the pus.
But the one over the sinus opening, which remains in place continuously, so that the pus contained within the sinus flows out. And so that you may know whether the pus flowing from the sinus is copious or scant, or digested or undigested.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus, if the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And when your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged over the sinus opening has already adhered, or is beginning to adhere, then on the third day, you should remove the adherence, and when you remove the dressing, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle virus quickly spreads, then do not despair of the adherence of it, for the healing of it comes about, for there are some people in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and others in whom the opposite occurs.
And if for many days undigested matter has been discharged from the sinus, then know that the sinus has not adhered; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you must adjust the drying properties of the remedies, and if the remedies are moist in their substance and their effect, make them dry, as a diaphanous ointment, when the superfluous addition of the astringent has been adjusted.
The final remedy, by which it is healed, is to take diaphanous ointment and dissolve it in rose-oil, and add some old wine that is strong in its substance, and mix it well with that, and use it.
And similarly in that action, when there is no pus, use diaphanous ointment, honey when it is cooked until thickened, and apply it; or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind it all into powder, and sprinkle it on the honey while it is on the fire, and then apply this to a cloth and apply it to the sinus; and apply this with the honey, which has been cooked until it is thickened, and sprinkle it on a cloth and apply it.
Sometimes, long aristolochia root and the root of the lily of the valley (and it is the lily of the valley), and the flour of erts and centaury are administered with it. Use these simple ingredients, or mix them, grind them, sieve them, and powder them over the honey I have described; use this.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the deeper part, and it is investigating the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then treat it with a fistulous cure, as stated above.
Of the treatment of felon, and bruised nails, and the incision of the added finger and sectioning (of it).
A felon is an abscess which arises beneath the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and often arises on other fingers.  And when it is prolonged and its treatment is less successful, or is an abscess of a hot abscess, or is corrupted, and it discharges pus until it rots the root of the nail, and often completely corrupts it, and often extends its corruption to the bone until it is foul-smelling, and the end of the finger is blackened, and its colour is dark.
And therefore, when you have done what we have said in the division and your treatment does not work, then you must cut away all the remaining excess of the nail with an iron tool, and then cauterize the wound nearby, for cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail's outgrowths are already pressing the flesh inward and beginning to sting and inflame it, then you should place a thin, sharp object under the edge of the nail that is stinging the flesh, and lift and cut that flesh easily, and apply something corrosive and burning to the remaining flesh until it is all gone; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut the bone out and remove it, for a diseased bone is never healed.
And if the decay and corruption spread to the finger, then you cut it at one of the joints, according to the likelihood of its recovery, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail has suffered a blow or friction, and there is intense pain, then you must first bleed the patient, and then dry the nail with a sharp instrument using a transverse cut from top to bottom, and be careful not to cut into the flesh underneath the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and it will be the cause of excessive inflamed flesh in the area; and then treat that area with whatever relieves the pain.
But a swollen finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is very light in the flesh, and is very light in the bone or very light in the nail.
And some of them are born at the base of the joint, or their origin is in certain joints of the fingers. But that which is born in certain joints of the finger is not harmful, and that which is born in the joints is very easily inflamed.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision should be gentle, and you should cut it away from its base using a broad blade.
But for that which is born at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, be careful of cutting it out.
But for that which is born on the finger near the joints, you should initially cut through the flesh in a circular fashion down to the bone, and then use one of those appropriate saws for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
But consolidation which occurs to the fingers (between their joints), whether by birth, or by the healing of a wound, or by a burn, or similar causes, then you must remove that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in warm oil to prevent rapid healing, and separate them, or place a thin lead sheet between them until it is healed as needed.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs on any finger with the palm, then you cut away that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable to the shape of the limb.
Of incisions of veins, these are varicose veins.
Varicose veins are large, twisted veins filled with melancholic superfluities that appear in various parts of the body, but particularly in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer purgation of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
However, the treatment of them with iron is in two ways: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other way is that the vein be elevated and all of it be extracted.
The cutting of it is done in this manner: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, foul-smelling blood from the leg is dissolved, then tightly bind the patient's leg from the upper thigh down to just below the knee with a bandage. Then, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Next, elevate the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg upward and downward, until the amount of blood you want and are able to remove has been evacuated, which will lessen the patient's ailment.
And then bind it up, and command the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this is noticed by the patient.
But the elevation of them is done in this manner: you raise the patient's leg, if it is very swollen, and then put it in a bath, and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins are swollen; or, if a bath is not available, it is exercised with strenuous activity until the limb is warmed.
And then cut the cord (possibly a vein or similar) on the right side of the vein, either lengthwise or behind the knee, or at the lowest part of the heel.
And then open the cord with a hook, and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears to the touch, and it is towards the appearance of that redness turning to blackness.
And then as you drain it, you see from the cord that white matter, which is the string.
And then you put a lead beneath it until it is raised and falls away from the cord, and suspend that with a hook or pliers.
And then cut another incision near that incision after three days, and then scrape the cord from the part above the vein until it appears.
And then lift that up and cut it in a second incision below the heel, and then pull it out and lift it until it is out through the second incision, and then pull it out of the incision above that.
Do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or furthest up of all the incisions, until all is out; and then cut that.
And if you are not able to pull and lift it out, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and tie it and pull it out, or put lead underneath it and turn your hand in all directions until it is thrown out. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to lift it out and it has become a detriment to the patient by that injury.
And when you have completely removed it, then place on the affected area wool soaked in rose-water and oil, both rose-oil and plain oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having twists and turns in which it is wrapped, and is not straight as we have said, then you must cut along every twisted and wrapped part, and then suspend it with hooks until you have lifted it entirely.
And be careful with your incision, that you do not cut the vein and harm it, for it will be difficult for you to lift it out; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which a vein is cut:
It is not so sharp as the hooks, nor does it have a sharp point, so as not to injure the vein; and it is of a thick, flexible shape, for if it were slender, it would cut the vein with its thinness; thus it has superfluous thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is produced in the legs in hot lands, like in Al-Andalus, in the lands of the Arabs, and in the hot, arid lands of India, where little vegetation grows and no trees are born.
And it is easily produced in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its formation is from putrefaction that comes from beneath the skin, like snakes, worms, and insects that breed between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the arrival of this affliction is a vehement inflammation in the leg; then the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, like the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap a piece of lead around it, weighing one drachma to two drachmas, and bind that and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
And as many times as there is any cause of it outside the parts, wrap that in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut away a part of it and bandage the remainder; and do not cut that at the root until it has come out, for if you cut it there, it is constricted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and possibly a bad ulcer in that place.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from behind it and no cause of it remains within the body.
And from this vein there have been cases of it being a length of five palms and ten, and it has even come to my attention of one that was twenty palms long.
And if it is incised during the time of its treatment, then insert the reed into its hole and pierce it with a long opening through the body until all that is in the matter has been evacuated, and apply a poultice to the area for several days with medicines; and then treat it by the cure for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and it comes to it with many orifices, and a branch comes out of each orifice.
Therefore, cure it in the manner we have described in the division and with what has been said above.
Of the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called the bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands "bovine illness" because it often affects livestock, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh, and it moves throughout the body, up and down, and is felt by its movement from limb to limb until it breaks through the skin and emerges.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, snakes, and intestinal worms and ascarids are generated in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it travels in the body and is elevated to the head, and reaches the eye, it causes a severe inflammation and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is only done by observing its movement and appearance as felt, and then you must tightly bind and constrict that which is above it and that which is below it, and then cauterize over it and extract it.
And if it is embedded in the flesh and you cannot find it, then you apply cautery with fire to the area until it dies, and the greatest fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the area, as we have said.
And if you see that creature having reached the head near the eye, then tightly compress the area above the eyebrow, and then cauterize over it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the sick person use medicine to cleanse their body of bad putrid humors, and guard against foods that cause putrefaction.
Of a section concerning the sickness (or ailment) of the nose.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is pain that comes in some members, and then it moves from one member to another.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm.  After I had stayed for an hour, I saw this inflammation move as a worm climbing up it, but more quickly than a worm might, like flowing, living silver that runs from place to place.
And then the pain moved to that location and settled into the muscle, and I remained there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other side of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then I noted that it moved throughout her entire body as I watched; and I was greatly amazed by its shifting from one part to another and the speed with which it moved. And I had already seen this before with my own eyes in this way, but I have seen many others where pain shifts from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes in quite the same way as in the woman.
And I did not think it was anything else than this, because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in this appearance the wind-like shifting became apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that this sort of shifting not appear in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, so you gently and lightly press upon it until that constrained windiness is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with purging of the body and with whatever expels windiness, and dispel those with foul-smelling pills and with pills of sarap and the like.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and according to the parts of the body they strike, for their diversity is such that some have large points, and small ones, and concave, and blunt ones; some are solid, others have three angles, some have tongues, and some have wings/feathers.
And the signs of the brain when an arrow strikes it, and pierces the bone, and exits through the skull which is over the brain, are sudden, violent pain, dizziness, vertigo, redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of thought, vomiting of bile, a difficulty in speaking and loss of voice; and a white, powdery moisture, like the moisture from flesh, oozes from the wound.
Therefore, if these signs appear, then you refrain from treatment and extracting the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast, are a feeling of a hard object being stuck in, not empty, and the arrow's movement feels similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the person experiences coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; then know that death is certain without a doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the coming forth of foamy blood from the wound, and the blood vessels near the neck become inflamed, the patient's color changes, the patient breathes heavily, and craves a draught of cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane, which is on the foot, the pain is then near the small ribs, and a large chill is produced with intense pain and sighing, and all the limb muscles are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then easily undigested food comes forth from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is embedded there, some opening from the wound reveals the intestine or tissue or a ruptured bowel, and there is no hope for a cure without removing the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine escapes from the wound, and some cause extends from it to the external parts, and intense pain afflicts the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders and arms, the spinal column, the collarbone, the hip, leg, and similar limbs, are saved according to the better disposition if the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you this, having seen the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand it for your own treatment.

Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the custom of phlebotomizing is described in chapter 16, are: two pulsating veins near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins in the two apparent temples; two veins in the two tear ducts, called *aspicientz*; the vein in the middle of the forehead; the vein at the end of the nose; two veins at the neck; two veins on the upper lip; two veins on the lower lip;  these are called *algelic*; and two veins below the tongue. However, the veins phlebotomized in the arms are five: one is the cephalic vein, located on the wild side [of the arm], and the people call it "the vein of the head," and it is quiet/still, a medial vein, and composed of a branch of the basilic vein and another branch of the cephalic; the people call that "the vein of the whole body."  The basilic vein is the one situated on the domestic side [of the arm], and is called "citillar," and the common people call it "the vein of the belly."  The vein of the forearm, which lies over the elbow, is the one usually phlebotomized, and the one that appears distinctly above the bend. The salvatella is the vein between the aforementioned auricular finger and the little finger, located in two bends/folds.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one is located below the bend of the knee on the wild side [of the leg]; the second is *sophena* and is located below the leg on the domestic side; and the sciatic vein, located below the leg on the wild side; and there are other similar veins in the leg.
The treatment of veins near the ears is for headaches, migraines, dizziness, and chronic head ulcers.
And the method of bleeding those veins, as I recommend to you, is to shave the patient's head, and rub the patient's head at the back of the head [occipital region], in the area of both veins with a wide cloth through good friction; then gently compress the patient's neck with a bandage until the two spots of the veins appear near the ears in two depressions at the back of the head; and you feel for them with your finger, and if you feel their pulse with your finger, then draw the blood with a lancet.
Then take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and carefully insert it under the vein along the groove until the phlebotomy reaches the bone. And then lift your hand with the vein and the groove upwards and cut the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be about the width of two fingers; and draw out as much blood as you want.
And then compress with pads and leave it until it is healed.
And already in the beginning of the book, the incision of those and their cauterization have been described.
But of the two arterial veins in the temples, the relief from phlebotomy is for old migraines, persistent and bad headaches, chronic ophthalmia, and discharges of acute excess fluids from the eyes.

And if you open a vein on the surface of the foot, then be mindful of the nerves that lie beneath it on the foot's surface, and perform your bloodletting in a way that cuts across, as if you were intending to make an incision.
And let the bloodletting be done carefully.
However, if the removal of blood is prohibited, then you should re-immerse the patient's foot in warm water and allow the blood to flow out until it is evacuated.
And if the blood-letter errs the first time, then repeat the bloodletting a little higher, because the location is safe; and do not fear harming him, but rather be mindful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly, do the same to the vein of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, beneath the bone of the outer side.
And the benefit of the bloodletting for him is for hip pain when it is caused by hot blood.
And the procedure for bloodletting him is that the patient enters a bath, and quickly and tightly wraps his thigh from the wide part towards above the heel with a long, thin bandage for four days, because it only appears this way.
And therefore, when it appears, then you perform bloodletting in that specific case according to whatever skill you have and according to the cross-section, and that is better; either cut it with an incision or cut it with a section, because the area is salty for him.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it does not appear in the sensation of all, then bleed a branch of it, and it is the one that appears on the back of the foot between the so-called auricular and annular [tendons/ligaments].
And beware of the nerves, and make the blood run the quantity you want.
And then loosen the constriction and place a cloth over the site of the bloodletting and compress the area, for it will heal quickly.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is of horns, or of earthenware, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping that is without incision is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping that is administered with incision and blood extraction are in fourteen places on the body, of which one place where cupping is placed is at the occiput, and it is the posterior part of the head; and the middle place of the two sides of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the jaw, then below the mouth; and cupping of the two shoulder blades; and cupping of the elbow, and it is above the end of the forearm; and cupping of the two armpits, and is of the two arms; and cupping of the two thighs; and cupping of the two calves.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore does not deplete their virtue, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping with or without fire be done for any illness caused by fullness until the entire body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping for illness or for a continuing condition, administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
And therefore, when any of these conditions occur, and especially if it's in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the neck is that it relieves heaviness in the head and what is pressing on the eyes; but this must be done after an evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of a phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it for those with cold, or those with a cold, for it causes great harm.
And therefore, it should not be used on the elderly, and on those with cold illnesses.
But for those who are continuously afflicted with it, it causes forgetfulness; and therefore, you should order the cupper to lightly hold the cup at the lower parts, to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alcohol is in place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica veins: and therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, and constriction of the chest and the cracking of the breathing apparatus, and coughs, and congestion.
And the cupping with alcohol should be positioned slightly higher, for if placed lower, it will engender weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* (likely a type of substance or preparation) is beneficial for accidental head pain, and for ophthalmia, migraine, inflammation, and toothache; and it is used in place of phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And you should tell the cupper not to press too hard with the scarification tool, so as not to cut the artery and cause bleeding.
And cupping below the chin is beneficial for ailments in the mouth, such as gum decay, and similar oral maladies, and is a substitute for phlebotomy of the veins located on both lips.
But cupping performed on both shoulder blades is beneficial for heatstroke, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two parts within the hollows/sockets is beneficial in place of three vein-openings, that is, the basilica, saphenous and cephalic veins, for it draws blood from all those veins, smaller veins that are in the flesh,  and these small veins from the larger veins, until the draw-in reaches the three large veins.
And tell the cupper not to proceed too far in the scarification, for the area is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping that is used on the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and the ulcers below.
And it is necessary to instruct the cupper to use a large, sturdy cupping glass, because the area needs a strong suction;  and flimsy, easily broken cupping glasses are weak and should not be used for scarification.
However, cupping of the legs is less beneficial because it depletes blood from the entire body, and it is helpful for old pains in the kidneys and bladder; it provokes menstruation and is beneficial for coughs and fevers; and it is a substitute for opening two saphenous veins and at that time, two other veins.
However, it greatly weakens the entire body and causes fainting in many men.
And the benefit of the cupping at that point is similar to the benefit of cupping on both legs.
And the quality of applying the cupping is to initially place the empty cupping glass and to create a moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping, but rather place them lightly, and leave them briefly, so that the humors come to the place by gradual accumulation. And do not stop repeating this and continuing until you see the area already reddened and swollen and that the redness of the blood appears.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction slowly, and then consider the disposition of the body's ailments.
For that person who has tender skin and is easily bruised, you should make incisions only once, so that the area is not ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the incision slightly, and to moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should make incisions twice, for in the first instance, the path of thinning the blood and its watery elements is opened. But in the second, the scrutiny gained from extracting the thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then the incision should be made a third time, so that the impurities are removed.
And finally, when we want to extract a little blood gradually or slowly, a single incision suffices.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know the blood is thick, then deep incisions are needed.
And the depth of the incision should be only the length of the lancet.
And if the skin over which the cupping is to be done is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, the areas for the cups should be oiled with softening, opening, and resolving oils; if it is summer, with almond oil, violet oil, or oil from soft berries, or quince seed oil; and if it is winter, with narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or bloodroot oil, or similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be of amaranth, or balm, or almond, or dill, and similar to them.
But if the area to be cupped has wide pores and tender flesh, then oiling is prohibited, and the areas around the cups should be washed with water or cool water of nightshade, or quince water, or burdock water, and similar to these.
And for those whose blood is of moist constitution, wash the areas around the cups with sour wine, or water of myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for those whose superfluities are thick, wash the areas around their cups with old wine, or water of tansy (which is majorana), or decoction of dill, or chamomile, and similar to these.
And the cupping should be guarded against bathing; having been administered shortly before bathing, for an hour or two, and the patient should rest after the cupping.
What is it that should be regulated before cupping or bloodletting?
Consider this at the beginning: if the person being cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflammatory blood, then they should take things that cool them down, such as fresh milks, vinegar with endive and lettuce, and secanabin, julep, and things similar.
