And for someone with a cold temperament, they should drink an althea syrup, or a syrup of alcaçuz seeds, and sumac, and a very fragrant wine between old and new; and they should be advised to consume little food and drink, and avoid foods like poultry, hempseed, pigeons, and small birds.
And on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more substantial than the food.
And some men should be given  triacle alpharac, or musk-based medications (i.e. musk or alcelita) before cupping or bloodletting, or shortly before, to strengthen the main body parts and thin the blood; and they shouldn't be given anything heating.
But cupping, without incision, should be applied to the liver, spleen, gout, and the navel, and on the knee areas, and the area around the groin.  For these parts cannot tolerate incision upon them.
And these are not intended for bloodletting from the body part, but rather to draw blood out of the body part, as we apply a cupping glass over the breast in cases of nosebleeds; and we use it to dissolve cold, stagnant air from a part of the body, as we do when placing it over the belly and the navel.  Because this action thins the body part, warms it, and relieves the pain because it resolves the stagnant air.
And it is placed on the sinuses when they are blocked or filled with dust, because by the virtue of strong suction it quickly clears blockages and eradicates dust from its place.
Similarly, it does the same when placed on the liver and spleen to remove any stagnant air contained within them.
And these cupping glasses are used empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are used with fire.
And sometimes they are used filled with lukewarm water in diseases of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and larger, it is either hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass used with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have formed it; and the quantity of the bottom be half a palm; and let it be on the patient's back, in the middle of the area to be treated, the hole being small enough for a needle to enter.
Let it be made of red earthenware or citrus wood, with thick rims, lion's, or other appropriate shapes, so that it does not displace the limb from its position on it.
And there should be a transverse rod of earthenware or iron in the middle, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping glass should be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one should be smaller depending on the illness and the age of the person using it.
The cupping glasses for children and the enfeebled are different from those for young people and those with strong bodies.
And the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the flame with suitable wood fire in front of a small beeswax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is ignited around the lower part, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it is placed on the patient's limb, and the finger is placed over the hole we mentioned, until when we have the cupping glasses and we don't want the finger to show or the vapor to escape through that hole, the cupping glass will immediately dissolve.
And then light the fire according to its method, and repeat it if necessary.
But a cupping glass administered for the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod nor hole, and is administered only by being filled with water, and is placed on the limb simply.
And this is the form of it: and the larger this cupping glass is, the better, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.

Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs in which cupping is not possible due to their small size, like the foot and the gums and similar things; or to a limb without flesh, such as the finger and nose and their like.

First Chapter.
Summary and collections regarding broken bones, and the prerequisites for understanding them, before we begin a review of fractured limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the principles, understanding of which is initially necessary for the craftsman and to stand on the truth of these; that is to say, you and those who are ready to learn or learn this noble art, other than yourselves.
And thus I say that when an accident of fracture, dislocation, twisting, or crushing occurs, then it is necessary that you begin with phlebotomy or a laxative, or both together, if no cause forbids it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the person affected is a child, or old and decrepit, or if it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And afterward, let their diet consist of cool herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit fatty meats and filling foods, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
And then return the patient to their initial regimen, with which they were accustomed.
And thus, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the patient be fed nutritious foods, rich, thick, and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, rice, porridge, boiled peas, cow's belly, eggs, fresh fish, and heavy wines and similar to these; and with this regimen, the fracture is more easily and better consolidated.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and those in poor health, in their first stages of illness, cannot be fully rejoined and healed naturally because of the weakness/debility of their limbs.
But nonetheless, nature unites and brings together over the broken bone from every side a certain glue-like substance, thickened, which adheres to and binds it until it is completely joined and fastened together, until it reaches its full strength and firmness, just as fire in the beginning, until some force prevents it from completing its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary to give the patient foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all the other limbs have different types of fractures.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
There is diverse types of fractures themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for a fracture may be curved, or it may occur in stars; and sometimes a fracture runs along the length of the bone: and those are the fractures of splinters, and stars, and additions proceeding and not proceeding, just as is done in the pen when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a crack and disruption in the head, and a small fissure fracture.
And for every type, there is a particular method or treatment for restoring it, according to the separate reminder in its places.
There are various signs of a fractured bone. And by this a fracture of the bone is known: it is the twisting of it, and the protrusion and appearance of it to the feeling, and so of it when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is it made by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a twisting or a small crack, so you should not move it with your hand and with a total compression; instead, place on it some of the remedies from which a restoration is remembered for that moment which is appropriate to the location; and then it should be tightened by a subtle constriction.
And know that when the location is bent but not split in two or otherwise affected, as if the entirety of its alignment is misplaced, then you must begin, from your time onward, to rectify it, lest an inflamed abscess occur there.
And if an inflamed abscess occurs there, then leave it for several days until the abscess subsides; and then dress it according to any method you can, by gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of this is easier than that of a bone in which a fracture occurs.
And then strengthen it according to what will be the remedy for it.
But if in the bone there is a fracture, then extension of this broken limb, whether the hand or the foot, is necessary; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and the hand.
And be your positioning in which you place the limb back into its natural shape, while the broken limb parts are extended; then you must carefully return those additions to their places with skill and in any way you can.
And take care, according to your strength, that your procedure does not cause further pain or injury to the patient; and be careful, with your skill, to apply one bone to its counterpart in the best possible alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you carefully feel it with your hand, and if you find any different cause, correct it and put it back into place with all your skill.
Avoid excessive stretching and compression, as some foolish doctors do, which often results in a painful abscess through their procedure, or causes the limb to contract, as we have seen from their procedures many times.
The appropriate level of immobilisation for the still limb is what I am explaining; so you will know that fractured limbs vary in size and form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and similar parts, the wrapping should be of fine, soft cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, a firm and strong wrapping is needed, as a strong bandage connects and firmly secures the large limb from every direction, ensuring no gaps or openings.
And then, after you've carefully aligned the limb, apply a suitable bandage to the fracture site, using three or four wraps depending on the limb's needs; and gently secure the bandage with your hand.
And then move the bandage towards the most prominent part of the fracture site, and secure it less tightly than you did at the fracture site itself. Then gently loosen the bandage around the fracture site a little bit, and gradually reduce the tightness, until you have moved the fracture site significantly.
Then take another bandage and wrap it once more over the broken area, and then move the bandages towards the lower part of the fracture; and your action should involve adjusting the tightness of the bandage and loosening it according to what we've previously described for the first bandage.
Then, between the layers of bandages, place some linen or cloth to address any twisting or deformity in the fracture if one is present; otherwise, place nothing there.
Then place another bandage over this, and immediately fasten these well-made bandages if there is no inflammation or abscess present in the area. If there is an abscess or inflammation, apply something that will relieve it and reduce the inflammation, leaving it for several days. Then tighten the bandages over the area.
The splints should be made of half-hardened wood, expertly shaped, or of pine wood, or palm fronds; and the width of each splint should be according to this shape and form.
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly wider and thicker than the others, and its length should correspond to the size and smallness of the limb; then, tighten the splints with another bandage, maintaining your first degree of tightness; afterwards, tie them together securely with thread as already described regarding the tightness, and the tightness should be greater closer to the fracture, and the further it is from the fracture, the looser the tightening should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some fools do, using thick, twisted ropes for thread, that is a great mistake, for it upsets the evenness of the binding.
Likewise, excessively fine threads are not suitable, because you won't be able to achieve the desired binding with them.  And there should be at least a finger's width between each splint.
And if the patient is complaining of the edges of the splints pressing on their skin, then place some soft material such as linen or padded wool under those parts, so as not to cause them injury.
Sometimes the fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a rupture of the ligaments may occur, and I will discuss this in detail in the second chapter.
And understand that not every broken limb should be bound with splints on the first day.  This is because when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it until approximately five, seven, or more days later, depending on your assessment of whether a dangerous inflammation (abscess) might develop.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A common plaster method for fracture repair, suitable for many constitutions, especially women and children, as it neither generates heat nor cold.  It involves taking powdered flour, specifically the inner pith of the flour that forms on the walls of a mill from the movement of the millstones.
And prepare it as it is, but sift it with egg white, and apply the mixture neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for treating fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  a recipe using asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild chamomile root, each one part.  Myrrh and aloes, each half a part;  aloes, two parts. Grind and sift all, and mix with a little water or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot. Then use this plaster, for it is a good composition and helps to heal slightly broken bones, and is effective for many people.
Another method of plaster for fractures and sprains:  a recipe using wild chamomile and white marshmallow root, each one part; myrrh and aloes, each half a part; acacia, one and a half parts; Armenian bole, two parts.
All are ground in a mortar with thorough crushing, sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and for accidents causing pain there, and for fissures and fractures: Take wool and soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but is effective for treating hot abscesses and relieving pain.
Another method of plaster for restoring a broken bone: take fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; crush them together and make a plaster from them.
A brief method of plaster for assisting in the restoration of a fractured bone, and when you want resolution of any remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, violet flowers, and comfrey flour, all in equal parts; crush all together, and mix with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
However, if it is inflamed, then mix it with damp coriander water or with cold water and apply it.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving a hard abscess that hinders the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow, linseed, bladderwrack, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all in equal parts; crush everything, and mix with willow water, or plain water, or wine, all according to the condition of the limb and the colour of the skin.
However, Hippocrates, in his book, does not say that it should be placed on the broken limb for its restoration, but rather a cerot (plaster) made of wax and oil, and no other substance; and recommends that it be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb for its restoration, and the substances in which there is drying and little warmth, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar to those substances mentioned, are used.
A plaster for wounds and pain: take equal parts of cheese, human skin (or bird skin), marshmallow root, and salt; crush and sift them, and make a plaster from the mixture.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain, and when it should be loosened, depends on whether pain, inflammation, or fracture or displacement of the bone has occurred.  If these are not present, then you do not loosen the bandage for many days. And if severe pain, inflammation, or swelling occurs at the site, then you should immediately loosen or remove the bandage, and take off the plaster.
And take linen and a damp sea sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water.  Wash the affected area with it until the inflammation subsides and the pain is eased.  Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.  Next, place a piece of wool soaked in sour wine and oil (or oil alone) on the area, and bandage it for a night, so you are certain the inflammation and pain have subsided.
Then repeat this gentle compression and small plaster application, and do not apply the initial strong constriction again.  Treat it gently until healed.
And if the abscess, inflammation, pain, and swelling are completely gone, and you no longer need the plaster and compression, then you should repeat the process as you did initially.
And if no such causes as we have mentioned affect the limb, then do not loosen the bandage for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes it is left for twenty days, depending on how the limb heals, as we said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh around it coagulates.
Then, increase the compression more than you did initially, and increase the patient's nourishment, according to the patient's recovery.
However, if you find the fracture site already drying out and weakening more than necessary, then understand that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, moisten it with lukewarm water every time you loosen the bandage; and do this for three days. Slightly loosen the compression, and through this action, nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal quickly.
But what those foolish bone-setters do, when a limb fracture isn't properly set at first, and it is set crookedly, is an error according to their procedure, a great risk.  If it were correct, the Ancients would have included it in their writings, and they would have done things this way.

Second Chapter: Of fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow with a weapon, and the blow breaks all the bone as far as the skull, which is below the bone, just as it does to a log.  Thus, this type is called a complete fracture; or the weapon cuts a portion of the bone and cuts the flesh, but does not extend through the bone; and this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the severity of these two fractures is either great or small, and a fracture that is a crushing or grinding; and the cause is a blow with a stone or falling on a rock, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane that is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the smallness of the fine hair-like fibers, and therefore this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is caused either by a fall or by a blow from a stone, and similar things, which causes some plate of the bone to enter into the interior parts, and is made in the hollowed-out place, as happens to the ribs of the body when they are struck, and a portion of the bone enters into the interior parts.
And this is more commonly found in the bones of moist bodies, such as the skulls of children.
And in all these types of fractures, the pieces are separated, like scattered stars. And I will come with a remedy for all of that, putting it back in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by examining them and investigating them with probes and by examining the decaying flesh that is on top of them.
A capillary injury is recognized because it is visible on the bone, and is wiped clean and inspected with a probe, because the fracture appears dark.
But in the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms, and if you see symptoms that indicate a serious concern, such as vomiting, agitation, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, swelling and stiffness of the limbs, and similar symptoms in the more severe conditions, without doubt the patient is not going to recover.
And if you see symptoms that are not severe, you expect recovery, then you begin the treatment.
The reason for this is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning of the injury, and this is in the winter days, then you must examine the bone before the 14th day according to all the conditions.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must try to set the bone before the 7th day, lest the tissue beneath the bone be corrupted by the pannus, and the symptoms we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and is with broken and crushed pieces, then you must cut away the broken and crushed part, as I will instruct you.
And you shall examine the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best as possible, given the nature of the injury, and be gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter a bone protrusion, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must treat it as appropriate.  Fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and roasted oil, and allow the injury to rest until the abscess subsides, and the blood flow stops; then begin the process of realigning the bone and removing the injury.
And this shall be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a straight-edged scalpel, and this is its form:  And then, apply another scalpel, a little further over, and this is its form:
And then apply another scalpel further still than the second one.
And you need a variety of scalpels, some wider than others; some shorter than others, with sharpened ends, made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking the scalpel so that you do not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is hard, then you should drill around it before using the scalpels with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
They are not called that because they do not reach the surface of the skull bone next to it, since the drill has a round end or rim on the one below the sharp head that resembles a small rim or circle, which prevents it from sinking into and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient for you to take a large number of these drills, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have drills for every part of the skull, with a sharp end, varying in length and width according to the thickness of each part.
And this is the method for three types of drills:  To drill around a fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone is penetrated; then change the drill to another location, and place it in a hole of roughly the thickness of a ray or similar width.
Then, using scalpels, cut what's between each hole in the bone, making it as fine as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with another tool, such as finely crafted pliers and forceps.
And you must carefully avoid letting the scalpel touch any part of the spinal cord.
And, having drilled around the bone, and if it's joined to the spinal cord, separate it, and remove it.  You should then scrape and smooth that area where it was connected to the bone using a tool similar to a scalpel, but thinner and more delicate than the others.
And if any small bone fragments remain, then easily remove them with your tools; then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
And another method of operation is gentle, devoid of fear, which the Gaulish authors praised highly.  And this is their teaching:  It is necessary for you to begin by uncovering the portion of the bone in the location of the most severe and obvious fracture, until you have uncovered that area and placed the tip of this slow and gentle scalpel beneath it; and this is its form:
And the lens-shaped portion is smooth and does not cut anything, and the pointed portion is bent in two directions forward along its length, like a V; so that the lens-shaped portion is aligned with the spinal column, and the sharp cutting edge is against the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on one side with a small mallet until you easily cut all the bone in such a way that you are certain the bone does not experience the accident you fear in any way, and if the surgeon were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of medicine; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Then if any bone fragments remain attached to the bone in some places, you will cut them away with the pointed tip of the scalpel; and will free it with ease, for it is freed without injury and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate the bone marrow, and what is cut away or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and small irregularities remain, then it is necessary to smooth out those roughnesses and eliminate those irregularities with small razors, for which you have prepared a variety of sizes, so that you can administer the most appropriate ones in every location and the most appropriate ones according to where your operation is on the fractured bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your process of smoothing the bone, you begin with the widest of those razors, and then gradually use the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you come to using the finest and most subtle of all those razors.
However, in each of the other small, hairline fractures, it is necessary to administer treatment according to what you perceive as necessary for their repair.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And this, what we have written, demonstrates [it] in large fractures.
And if the flap of skin is retracted when you reduce the bone, then it is necessary to take a cloth equal in size to the wound, soak it in warmed olive oil, and place it over the wound opening; then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in warmed wine and olive oil, and place this over the first cloth; and do this as gently as possible, so as not to aggravate the condition; then apply a wide bandage, and do not tighten it except to the extent necessary to hold the wound closed.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain of avoiding a suppurative inflammation; then remove the dressings, and then administer medicines that are beneficial for dryness, such as flaxseed and the flour of barley, and the powders of anise, and of arnica, and similar things.
And you should make these medicines into a powder, pulverized and applied to the wound as they are dry; and universally, administer any medicine whose properties are to cleanse and not to burn.
And be mindful, through your skill, that the wound remains clean in your treatment, and do not allow the application of ointments or other substances that could cause a worsening of the wound.  For when virulence is added to the skin covering the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and thereby brings about a very wicked affliction in the patient.
And if damage occurs to the brain's structure, especially when the treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if you see these symptoms, which we have described, you know that the condition is dangerous without any doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the wound itself, but rather from the medicine applied, and it is the nature of that medicine to cause this blackness, then you should take a portion of honey and three parts of rose-water, and with this, moisten a cloth, and place it on the head; and then treat it with the appropriate remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in just one part but sometimes in two parts, or even sometimes in one of those parts, because it has two bones.  For the lower portion is cartilaginous, and it's not broken, but rather only shows bruising, twisting, and bending.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into the nostril, and use your index finger to push the fracture inward, and your thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this easily, and be careful not to cause further pain to the patient through your actions.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and your finger doesn't reach it, then it must be set with the end of a rounded, slightly thickened stick (or tool).
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same.
And try to restore it on the first day after the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately on the 7th or 10th day you should treat the inflamed area carefully.
And approximately insert a small piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part, or place two small pads if the fracture is in two parts, and let the padding be of such a size to fill the nasal opening.
Some of the ancient healers say that the pads should be soaked in broth and changed daily, but I do not see the need for this.  Instead, the cloth should be dipped in a mixture of beaten egg and powdered medicine;  and leave the pad in place until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
Sometimes, instead of a pad, a piece of linen cloth, soaked in a healing liquid, is placed in the nose, especially if the nose fracture is severe and the patient is unable to breathe normally.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, do it with pads.
And if, on another occasion, there is a suppurating boil, plaster the nose with a cerot (ointment) or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil, or with diachylon.
However, if there is no suppurating boil, then you should cover the external area with flour of sesamum and powdered herbs mixed with egg white; and then place a linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are fractured, even slightly, or are comminuted, then you must cut away the surrounding tissue and remove the pieces with appropriate instruments.  Then close the wound and treat it with healing and consolidating remedies, and appropriate plasters.
And if a pustule develops inside the nose, then you should treat it with pads and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is without pus, observe if the fracture is merely displaced, and not broken into two pieces but bent inward at the edges, then its repositioning is straightforward.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the aforementioned index finger of your right hand; and push the bent portion of the fracture inwards easily, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; using that, gently straighten it.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to administer extension to both parts according to a straight line until its possible realignment.
And if you encounter any tooth damage or displacement, then firmly secure those you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a compress on the fractured jaw, and then place folded cloth over that, and place a large, well-made bandage or a piece of linen matching the jaw's length over the cloth; and then bind that above it in the manner best suited to your method and application, until it is no longer loose.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and provide him with easily digestible, light meals.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; and then straighten what has been altered and plaster it with ground powder, egg white, or fine flour, after you have removed the bandage and place a soft wool plaster on top.
Then, once this plaster is firmly attached and the bone's position is not further altered, leave it, do not remove it until the fracture is healed and firmly set; for often this fracture is set within three weeks.
And if a harmful abscess should occur again, then administer what we have repeatedly mentioned for its treatment until it is healed and the abscess has subsided.
And if the fracture is with a scab, then consider whether it's a detached piece of bone or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for their removal from the instruments.
And if the opening of the scab is narrow, then you should widen it with leeches, according to your needs.
And once you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains, then you should examine the opening of the scab, if it is wide; and if not, apply one of the suitable plasters to it, and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Often the collarbone is fractured in the part nearer to the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is fractured, or separated, or separated into two parts, apart from splinters; and it is easy to restore; or it is such that splinters occur, and it is difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is with a scab.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a scab, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the support and steady the fractured collarbone, and the other extends the shoulder towards that part.
And then gently manipulate the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, as it should be, neither protruding too much nor concave.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, of a size appropriate to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone, and apply pressure to the pad with your hand until you align the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone ends outward, because they have become fixed in their position, then the patient should be placed on their back, and a cushion of moderate size should be placed over the muscle of their neck.
And the assistant should press on the muscle on either side beneath, until the collarbone is lifted from the position in which it is fixed, and then you should correct and manipulate the fracture with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are loose, then you should divide the splinters and easily remove them.
And if it is lodged within the bone, then you should make an incision there with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this purpose, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument that serves as a lever; and it is an instrument of wood or iron, of which this is its shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as possible for the operation; it should have two ends, like the letter "V", and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the rupture of the wound that you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are certain of a suppurative inflammation, then you should close the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a suppurative inflammation, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the extent of the wound's incision.
And if suppurative inflammation occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rubbed oil and wine, and place it on top.
And take care that the splint is not moved from over the broken bone.  Therefore, you should see the patient every day, and when you see the ligaments softened or the splint already loosened, then you should adjust it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's head on a pillow, and put a small cushion under the patient's armpit beneath the pillow at night.  So that with this, the patient's arm is elevated away from the side, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone is elevated from the muscle; or the patient's arm is tied to the patient's neck.
Do not loosen the bandage if there is not inflammation or suppuration in the area for up to twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the patient's condition allows, and adjust the splint and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and coagulated.
And it is tightened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is fractured in the lateral part of it, and it is not broken through but only the edges.
And therefore, when the place of it is fractured – and it is fractured in the middle of it – and it is not known except by touching, then according to the appearance of the fracture, prepare it and return it to its natural shape using all possible engineering techniques.
Then place on the area powdered chalk mixed with egg white and stop it with linen; then place a folded cloth poultice; and then place a thin, flat wooden board over it, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
However, if underneath the board there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with linen, and then raise the board so it is level.
And then bind it tightly with a long strip of cloth, with appropriate constriction and firmness on the board, until it is held securely in place.
And check the bandage every day; and whenever it softens, bandage it again, and tighten and adjust the board if it has shifted from its position.
And let the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days is completed, then you loosen the bandage, and you will know for certain if the injury is of the bones, whose connection is not feared, nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the heavy bone and place the splint under it, then you cut around it, remove it, and do as we have said in the treatment of a broken collarbone or abscess, should such occur here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fractured foot.
Sometimes the foot is fractured in the middle, and that heals late; but the extremities heal more quickly because they are jammed and crushed.
And the symptoms when it is fractured in the middle are that it declines towards the inside parts, and great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, strong fever and vomiting of blood occur; and the cavity in the broken bone is apparent, and not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be laid on their back, and a cushion be placed between their shoulders; and then press on the muscles, and adjust the bones with the hands on both sides, and be subtle in the operation and in the straightening according to every possible manner and that which is appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place a poultice and bandage on it, and place a thin splint of willow or reed or similar lightness on top, then wrap it in cloth; and then gently bind that around the broken bone to prevent it from moving; and apply a bandage that is rounded on the back sometimes, and tighten it with appropriate tension; and then check the bandage constantly, and when it softens, tighten it further.
And if the need arises for loosening it due to festering, swelling, pain, or abscess that arises in the area, then you should loosen and remove it, and remove the poultice, and rectify what occurred with the appropriate treatment; then you should reapply the poultice, if necessary, and adjust the tightness until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in the thick parts, that is, the middle; and the extremities of their edges (or ends) do not suffer bruising (or injury) because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the senses through investigation with the fingers.
And the restoration should be done by aligning the fracture with the fingers in the best possible way, until it is properly joined, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if needed.
And if the rib fracture is towards the inner parts, the patient suffers intense pain and stabbing, like the stabbing pain that occurs in pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is a difficult condition to cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in dealing with that.
And from what they said:  it is necessary to give the patient foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is swollen and distended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
However, we should avoid this, as it is not a light cause to generate a hot abscess if it does not occur; and if it does occur, then it will help with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on it and strongly drawn; and it seems reasonable, but nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass draws the superfluities to the area due to weakness.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, and the dressing should be applied around the affected area according to its shape; and then the patient should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause afflicts the patient, who cannot endure it, and the bone pierces the membrane with a painful wound, we fear for the patient, then we must cut open the area, expose the broken rib, and then place an instrument on it to keep the shape, the memory of which has preceded, and cut the bone easily and remove it.
And then, if the wound is large, we should stitch together the two edges of the wound, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie on his back on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the spinal column of the back and neck.
Sometimes there are fractures in the bones of the neck, and there are also often injuries to them, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you often find their muscles already softened, numb, dead, and you cannot move them, stretch them, or tighten them, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in this state of things, it is fatal.
And if you move them and feel constriction with your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's marrow is intact and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar signs to this on the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will heal or not, then examine the patient's feet.
Then, if you find them already softened, and notice the same symptoms as those in the muscles, and when they lie on their back, they experience involuntary windiness and urination, and sometimes they are lying on their back, wanting to urinate but unable to, then know that it is fatal, and do not attempt treatment.
But if you do not observe any of those symptoms, the cause is milder and easier to treat.
And the treatment for this is to apply a sedative poultice to the inflamed vertebra, either with oiled or roasted herbs, or with beaten egg whites; leave this on for a few days until the inflammation subsides.
And then apply a comforting and stimulating plaster, and bind it firmly; and instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the affected area until it heals.
And if you observe a separation of the bone, or any other cause that has already separated it, then it is necessary to dissect along the fracture line and remove that bone; then bring the two sides of the wound together, with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with a healing salve until healed.
But if the back of the bone is fractured, and it's the end of the shaft, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and realign the fractured bone with the other hand as much as possible by means of a second-stage reduction; then apply a plaster and bandage, if needed, and secure it.
And if you find fractured splinters there, then you should remove them and treat the wound with the previous treatment until healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] width [or breadth].
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and if they break, their fracture is only a separation at their extremities, and they are cut lengthwise and deflected inward;  the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it so that it is over the fracture which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you shall adjust it so that it is properly aligned until it is assembled into its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along the length or already inclines towards the lower parts, then you must have the patient lie on their stomach so that you are prepared to restore that fracture. And then, once you have adjusted it, place a poultice over it, and then place a splint of wood or bone over that, and bind tightly so that you need not fear puncturing the fracture or displacing the splint.
And adjust the hollow with that which fills it until it takes on the tightness according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side.
But if a festering abscess occurs, then cease the extension and restoration until the festering abscess is soothed, and apply that which soothes it as is previously described; then resume the restoration, and bind it as is appropriate.
And if the bones are shattered or comminuted, then you must set them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of the fractured adjunct.
Adjutori is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness in this manner:
And two ligaments are tied to its two ends, and it is then suspended from a raised location.
And the patient lies down on a bed; and then place the patient's broken arm over the wood until it reaches the point where the curve of the wood is attached to the middle of the area.
And then suspend something heavy on that, or the attendant extends it downwards; and then the doctor holds the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
The other method is that the patient is placed on their back and the hand of their neck is suspended with a bandage; and then command two attendants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other holds what is below it; and each of them pulls in their direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you bind the fracture with a bandage and another bandage over that, and each of the two attendants pulls in their respective directions.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the point and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary that the bandage be placed over that area and around the elbow.
Apply that with ease and without trouble, and then, once it is bound properly and applied with good application, tighten it if there is no danger of a purulent abscess developing.
But if an abscess forms, then leave the tightening until the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the abscess subsides, and then you can tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster over the fracture, then place a wrapping of clean cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm against that support, and place their open hand over their own muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the support around the arm so that the arm is in the proper position, unless some cause from the broken bone prevents this.
And then use splints.
And you should place on the fracture the splint that is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the tightening method I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your tightening around the fracture site is more tight. And the longer the fracture, the less tightening there should be.
Therefore, adjust the positioning of the splints and the tightening as we have said, at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do so.
And if you fear a deep abscess, then you leave the splint and the strapping in place for seven days, as we have said.
And now, consider the ligament in all its parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs at the site; or, because excess tightening prevents food from reaching the limb; therefore, correct all this according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place a full, wool-lined poultice over the affected area, and be aware at all hours of the night or day that the fractured limb's shape is not weakened or loosened, or that the ligament is softened, and therefore rectify all of this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the previous order, so that the food is initially light until the swelling begins to subside; and then it is necessary to increase the food's strength.
For according to custom, the affected area and leg should be bandaged for 40 days; and then they should be loosened, and a bath should be given, and medicated with ointments that correct it.
And if the fracture is severe/complicated, then you should not loosen the ligament and bones from that injury for up to a month, or even up to two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones; of which one is small, and it is the one that sits by the said wrist, and the other is large and is the one located below the small wrist, on the lower part.
And often the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and often both are broken together.
And when the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is more straightforward.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, the fracture is severe, and the healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the broken bone is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is appropriate for the physician, in restoring it, to apply a small extension with ease until it is healed.
But if the larger forearm bone is broken, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful extension.
And if both forearm bones are broken together, then it is necessary for you to apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you position the hand's figure for restoring it and extending it on a cushion, and let the palm of the hand be higher than all the other parts mentioned, and the little finger be lower than the other fingers, and let the patient be lying on a smooth bed.
And let a cushion be in front of him at his height or in line with him, so that the patient does not experience discomfort.
And then the attendant should extend the lower arm with their hand or bandage, and another attendant should extend the upper arm similarly.
And then the physician should manipulate the bone until it returns to the best possible position.
And if, in setting the bone, there are obstacles, then you must use your skill to return every piece to its proper place.
And if there appears a separated fragment that punctures the skin and is not to your satisfaction in restoring it, then you should operate on it and remove it according to the method mentioned above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of that.
And if you encounter a suppurating abscess at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with a plaster made of roasted oil and white wax, and it should be in a mixture of subtlety and fineness, and tightly bind the cloth over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a prepared poultice of powdered mold with egg white, and then place plasters; and let the plaster placed over the fracture be as wide and strong as possible.
And understand that the names of the plasters for the arm are found in the major arrangements, or that is, the fracture in one compartment or in two compartments together.
And then apply your binding to the fracture site as strongly and forcefully as possible, and the more the binding is applied to the upper parts of the fracture or the lower parts, apply the binding a little more loosely according to the remembrance of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of a medium thickness, between thin and thick, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and its ligaments for some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as swelling occurring in the limb, then you must immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling ceases.
And leave the limb unbound until it rests, and then reapply the binding.
And if the binding has softened, and the bone is now realigned and resembles the others, then correct all of this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, for then you must slightly loosen the binding and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not exhibit any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen the binding for approximately 20 days or thereabouts.
And then, through their own efforts, the patient's hand should be suspended, and they should sleep on their side.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or very easily in 28 days, and all this depends on the disposition of the body's complexions and the strength of the body.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand's wrist and fingers.
Know that the wrist of the hand and the joints of the fingers are frequently fractured, and not only injured through blows but also often through friction.
And when the hand is injured by fracture or friction, then it is necessary for the patient to be lying down, and before him, a bandage is placed evenly, and then the patient's hand is placed on that bandage.
And then the attendant extends the broken bones, and the physician holds them until they are firmly joined together.
And then it is necessary to place a poultice and bandages, if not a boil occurs; and then a splint is placed over the affected area, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, and then make a sort of sling of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; afterward, it should be bound with long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather with cloth on which it is raised, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and bind it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint over it and another splint from the lower part into the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply a bandage, as if the hand is turned to the fingers by ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bound as necessary, and then it should be held with the palm, and if you wish, put a small straight splint on it, so that the fracture is raised and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the pinky finger, then it should be bound and tied with the finger that lies beside it; or all of them should be tied together in sequence, and that is better; or place a small splint on it, just as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and then after that, the arrangement of a cautious and opposing abscess—with your operation—with whatever is necessary when any of those causes occur, as the narrative of it is repeated.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken, and is declared to be broken according to the part that is converted to the front and to the most superior part.
And the restoration of it is done so that the ligament is tightened over the fracture, and another ligament under the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and on their face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its proper part, according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be near the knee, in the direction of the hip joint, so that the extension is above it, and the other ligament under the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the ligament should be near the knee, and the extension should be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is joined by a good connection; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, otherwise the affected limb will become infected. However, if it becomes infected, then you leave it for a few days until the infection subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this:  wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, folding it two or three times, removing any excess; then wrap the leg until you reach the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, and let the ends of the splint extend from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints over the thigh, at the location of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the gaps between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is consolidated.
And then start with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and as you move away from the fracture site, your bandage should be less tight, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until they reach the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side to prevent the bandage from shifting out of place.
And then leave the bandage on, ensuring that no inflammation, abscess, or swelling or similar conditions affect the limb; and if any such problem arises, remove the bandage and correct what has happened immediately, as we have often said.
But if there is a piece of bone that has come loose, then you must reposition it as best as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it, and treat it with what preceded the remedy until it is healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened with splints, just as we have said for the shoulder and arm.
However, in the restoration done in this way, the patient does not experience limping; and if it is restored alone, or if the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one who is always limping should do so.
And know that the thigh is bandaged for 40 days, or a little more, or a little less, and all this is according to the variety of constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fractured kneecap (or patella).
Know that a fracture of the kneecap often heals, but often results in a rubbing (or abrasion).
But if a fracture occurs, it is then only a crack or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a rupture, and concerning all this there has been a feeling (or sensation).
And the restoration of it is to bind together what is broken in its parts with bandages until it is joined and conjoined as much as possible by means of equability (or evenness), ease, and construction.
And then apply a plaster and place it over the affected area, if needed; and bind it firmly with suitable bandages.
And now consider all the methods we have described for other fractures, similarly to how an abscess is treated and similarly to how one would oppose any accident with what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the femur; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And the treatment for any type of fracture of these two leg bones is similar to the treatment for fractures of the arm, and therefore the restoration is also the same, and the operation for it is one and the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is displaced in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is displaced anteriorly (or forward).
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it underneath, and that is amongst the things not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and bandaging of the splints accordingly.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and ease it with your skill.
And if there is any need for an operation on the arm in conjunction with the leg, and when you have aligned the splints and completed your operation on the leg, then you will take wooden splints (or boards) placed under the supporting structures, which are made from palm branches; take ones with a smaller diameter, and do not use any that are too thick, and make them the length of the leg, from the knee down to just below the ankle.
And then wrap each splint in two layers of cloth according to its length, placing one over the leg on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle.  By this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a splint has been administered, corresponding to the length of the leg; and the leg should be supported so that it retains its position.
And this is especially necessary in cases of fracture, and when the bone is broken properly.
And from then on, examine the leg daily and study it thoroughly, and watch for abscesses, inflammations, or other similar issues.  If any such problem arises, apply the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel bone, and the bones of the foot and toes.
Fractures of the heel bone do not occur frequently, but fractures of the foot bones do happen sometimes; and the toes of the foot rarely fracture, instead often only showing friction or abrasion damage in their usual positions.
And if the bones of the foot do fracture, and those bones are displaced from their proper positions, then the patient should place their foot on the ground in a way that is carefully aligned like when walking.
And then place one foot on something that supports the displaced bones, and then press and press down on them, and compress them until they return to their proper positions.
Then apply a plaster or bandage on top, and place a small board with two flat ends underneath the sole of the foot.
And then firmly bind that with a downward-pulling bandage, after you have wrapped it in cloths and other materials as needed.
And when three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandages, for you will find the bones either compressed, or broken, or dislocated, and the treatment in those cases is as you know it.
But if some bones are broken, then repair them, and compress them in the same way as I have described and recounted to you in the repair of fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and slightly wider than it; and then place the board underneath the foot, as previously described, and bind it with a downward-pulling bandage.
But if the broken bones are two, three, or more, then place splints of appropriate size, wrapped in linen, on each finger; and bind the board to the lowest part of the foot, and have one end of the board extending above the patient's belly, so that it holds it firmly in place.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes I have mentioned in the other fractures and accidents we have discussed; and be prepared for every accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of fractures of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis in men.
When the thigh bone is fractured, then place that leg in a well-fitted splint, and gently incline it towards the back a little, and it should be held behind.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be fitted to the thigh in such a way as to support it; then the woman should be kept still, and slowly raise her back, as this should be done carefully until the fractured part of the thigh is aligned and the broken bone returns to its place.
And then place cushions under her back, and when she wants to urinate, she will remove the wool easily, and then she urinates; then you reduce and return it in the way that you put it in place at the beginning, and then return it to its original cushion.
And do this for seven days or so, until it is healed.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and fit a reed tube over the wound; and place the entire bladder inside the thigh, and then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh, for this will help the fracture to return to its proper position.
And then fill it with wool and leave it for several days, as we have described, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have described for the hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby and less severe fractures, especially for those who study and use and understand my words in this book; for this fracture signifies the best methods for restoring and healing it, and thus understand well.
However, when a man's penis is broken, take a bandage of wool and place it on the penis; then place a wrapping of cloth over it, and let it remain wrapped for three days or so, and it will heal.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when affected by plague.
It is advantageous for the one who suffers a fracture affected by plague, and especially if the bone is large, such as the bone of the thigh or the upper arm, or similar to those, that you should bleed and leeches him/her immediately if the conditions for bloodletting permit or indicate as we have said above.
And if the plague causes the outflow of blood, then you must apply a styptic to it, in such a way that you powder it with crushed zegi, if no other remedy is available.
And then begin restoring the fracture on the same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a serious abscess occurs, then you should delay the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days, for you will cause harmful accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the uncovered skin, then you must carefully reduce and hold it in place with your hands with ease and little stretching.
However, if your hands are not prepared for the stretching and repositioning of the bone, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the injury.  Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, depending on the amount needed for treatment of any kind of fracture.
And it should be round, and of little thickness, so that it is not bent by the pressure on it during the operation.
And it should have sharp edges at its ends; and the top should be thick, tapering to half its size towards the ends.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", which means a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sharp end be placed elevated on the prominent bone end.
And you push it in one go until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some sort of equality/evenness.
And thus, strive to align the fractured ends, and if the fractured end is delicate and does not accept the instrument properly, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument has sufficient purchase.
But if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have recounted, then you should cut it with the appropriate instruments for cutting, which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible.
And then remove whatever rough edges and small fragments remain on the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not been returned to its natural place; and then do what you deem to be of great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then immerse a cloth in black astringent wine, and if it is summer, specifically, do not put a plaster or any other oily substance on the wound, so that it does not cause festering and corruption.
And after administering remedies at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, leave the wound uncovered, and cut the wound with scissors, and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to squeeze the wound against the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or debilitating illness upon their patients, which is to say, gangrene. And if your (treatment) is light, gentle, different from the treatment of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear some of the bad accidents we have described, and you find pain in the area, then it is not appropriate to put plasters there, but rather make a bandage of hard cloths instead of plasters, and bind it tightly with those.
And after about a day or two, once the wound begins to fester, if pus is generated there, then you remove the cloth you had placed over it with wine, and then apply some ointments, with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage and consider the wound every day morning and evening until it is healed and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus runs from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound has passed many days, and it is not healed nor is the pus separated from it, then know that there are signs of complications here, for which you must probe the wound with a probe. And of those signs, those that are separated remain and are extracted; those that are not separated and cause pain in the limb, force you to cut them if possible.
And if the wound acquires a discharge or a swelling, i.e., symptoms, or another type of corruption or putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply to each of those what is appropriate for its cure, which is preceded by a description in its chapter.
And regarding this, where my sermon should end and you should present your skill: when a bone is broken, treat the limb as you would the thigh bone and similar to those of the larger limbs, and do not attempt to pull or remove it, for often death results; leave it until it sets, for it will likely set on its own after about 20 or 30 days. Then treat the wound, if you see fit, and if it doesn't set, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of swelling that occurs in the area of any fracture.
Often this swelling occurs in the area of healing from a fracture, especially if it's near the joints, and it disfigures the limb's shape; and it severely impedes the limb's natural function.
Therefore, consider if the swelling is recent and apply medicines that are astringent, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, and ceruss and similar ones;  take some or all of them, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply that mixture to the swelling on a cloth, and tightly bind it.
And leave that bandage firmly in place for several days; and then remove it and apply another, until the swelling is gone; or bind a lead plate on top of the swelling, as lead has the property of reducing swelling in the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already hardened and become tight and needs to be removed forcefully, then cut it at the most prominent part, and remove the excess and protruding part, or cut it with certain rasps until it's removed; and treat the area until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is healed, and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, this is often due to several causes: one being the excessive loosening or incorrect binding of the ligaments; or an excessive tightness of the ligaments that prevents nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive use of poultices; or excessive movement at the wrong time; or a lack of blood in the patient's body and their general weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give the patient food and make their body soft until blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce joy and happiness to them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses, until the nourishment flows and the limb returns to its natural shape.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of broken bones when they are repaired crookedly and the correct procedure is not followed.
When you see the restored limb displaying crookedness, prominence in the fractured bone, and a lump, and this is therefore a deformity of the limb, but nonetheless the limb is not prohibited from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of someone who claims that the limb is now newly broken.
But many foolish physicians and bone-setters have done this in our land; and this procedure is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
However, if the limping and lumping are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which softened herbs are steeped, such as mallow leaves and its roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and apply a softening poultice, like a well-made diachylon; or prepare a mucilage from mallow root and mix it with henbane juice or beeswax oil, and apply that; or use ripe figs, crush them with pigeon droppings, and similar medicines that are known to promote healing.
And sometimes it is resolved with diligent, gentle rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become strong, hardened, and difficult to cure, then it is necessary to stop the pus and dissolve the continuation of the bone, and carefully cut away the excess swelling around the bone with sharp instruments.  Careful and diligent treatment should be administered.
Chapter 23.
Concerning (or, On) Dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, preventing movement, deforming the limb, and causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident causes a dislocation, it is essential to quickly try to relocate it, and do not delay its reduction, for if delayed, the area becomes inflamed and hardened, making the dislocation difficult to reduce.
And therefore, it is not appropriate to manipulate or stretch during the inflammatory stage, for often it causes spasms and worsening pain.
But when this happens, you should resort to bloodletting the patient, and leave it until the inflammation subsides; then, the limb should be soaked in warm water and oil, and then reduced with ease.
And every limb is treated in this way, so that the memory of the joint returns to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, following the procedure for fractures; from the highest part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of a dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are displaced from their places by a slight movement, and slightly softened; or they are completely and fully dislocated, until they are softened at the joint, to the point that the patient is drooling, and cannot keep it, or cannot close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped up in his mouth.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete and full, then a gentle reduction must be administered, and it must not be delayed at all.
And the assistant should hold the patient's head, and the doctor should insert the tip of one hand into the dislocated jaw's socket inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he should insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he should hold; and he should command the patient to stop the movement of his jaws and to disengage them from each side; and the doctor should pull and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And thus, when they return and are held together, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over them poultices of cloth with cerate, and it should be made of wax and rubbed oil; and then it should be bound easily with soft bandages.
