And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not turn to the right or left side; and do not force him to chew anything; let their food be soft and easily digestible until the pains have subsided and the jaws are healed, then let him eat what he wants.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not trouble opening the mouth for eating, drinking, or breathing until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fever and persistent sweating occur, and the patient's stomach is quite easily upset, and there is much vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and often the one who experiences this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from within the chest by its connection to the feet; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is apparent to the senses.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then tightly compress the area with your palm, for it will return.
And then apply a poultice and powders to that area and compress it.
However, the extremity that is connected to the elbow and continues from there, is not dislocated in any way; therefore, it should be reduced and healed according to what we have said and as you are equipped to do.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and bandages, and command the patient to adhere to quietness and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated in the upward and downward direction.
And the second is that it is dislocated in the part above the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the parts behind by the shoulder, and it is not dislocated to the parts in front by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out to the lower parts towards the neck, and most often in those whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated, but in those whose flesh is thick, it is difficult to push out.
And often, accidents befall some men through blows or falls, and the muscle of the sufferer is swollen with a hot abscess, and it is thus believed to be dislocated.
Therefore, you must test this until you are quite certain of it, and then you proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts towards the neck, so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a hollow at the head of the muscle and below the neck near the touch of the head of the muscle, just like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it with any movement.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear point of contact, and it is not hidden.
And a slight reduction of this dislocation is made when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the attendant raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; and then places your fingers below the patient's neck, and lifts the joint with strength back to its place, and the attendant raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it down because it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a long time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and given an application that softens and smoothes, like if it were a mixture of the roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and this should be administered.
Then the patient should be laid on their back, and under the neck a wool pad should be placed, with a degree of looseness and firmness, and then the physician places their heel on the pad, and lifts the muscle head with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not pulled downwards, and another attendant holds the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, because the joint will promptly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the opposite side, and put his muscle under the patient's neck and lift the patient's neck upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another attendant pulls the patient's hand from the lower parts of their body. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down, because the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced by another method: a long post is placed in the ground, its top rounded like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and placed under the patient; then lions' cloths are placed on top of the post's head, and the patient stands along the length of the post, and then the patient's hand is extended downward, and the body is pulled outward with force, because the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a post whose length is two arms' length and whose width is four fingers, and its thickness should be suitable for the neck in this way:
And then lions' cloths should be tied to the rounded head so that the post does not injure the patient.
And then, a support is placed under the patient's armpit, and the hand or arm is extended along the post to the lower parts, and the post is tied to the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a ladder rung according to its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the other body part is suspended from the other side, so that the joint quickly enters its proper place.
And when the repositioning is complete according to any method possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a wool pad of sufficient quantity; then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with honey and egg white over the entire muscle, thus covering it, and then compress under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be wound over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to the neck, and left still, and the hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be limited until the limb is strengthened, because this is more conducive to their recovery.
And then, the diet should be increased gradually to seven or five portions, and some movement should be encouraged.  Thus, if it is firm and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated frequently due to moisture that affects it or another cause, then in that case a cautery with three openings should be administered, as is previously stated in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and bandage are repositioned several times, and it is still not firm, and the arm cannot be raised above, then understand that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are now cut, extended, or softened, and know that the joint will never return to its proper place.
For dislocations occurring near the foot and the nipple or the back parts, reduction is accomplished by pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area, and treat it until it is healed.
And if after healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and the limb returns to its original condition.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and equally difficult to return to its proper position; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so towards the front than the back.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is apparent under observation or touch, whatever the specific configuration of the dislocation.  And when you align the dislocated elbow with its proper position, this is clearly revealed to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can the arm touch its own muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes a deep-seated abscess, for if it becomes a deep-seated abscess, the reduction is difficult, and the limb is not entirely healed; and more so when it is located in the back areas, for it is the worst of all other types of dislocations, and causes the most pain, and often leads to death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand is extended with two hands, and the arm is outstretched, and the physician's hands are on the elbow above and below it, and the physician pushes the joint with the tips of their fingers together, or with the heel of their palm, until it returns to its proper place.
But if the dislocation is towards the front, then it is returned by bending the arm with their hand, until the physician strikes the elbow with the heel of their hand.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a forceful extension, very strong.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension so that it does not revert to its previous position during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, since it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over their hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil, so that this lubricates the joint and makes it easier; and then push the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns to its proper place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that it be placed on a poultice in which there is astringency and a binding effect, using egg white, and be bandaged tightly downward, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be untied; and therefore, if the joint is formed in its proper place, then you loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not properly tightened, then you reapply the poultice and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then untie it.
But if there is a serious complication after the joint reduction, and the patient's condition is unstable, then apply moist heat, gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place on the joint a soothing, fatty substance; and then bandage it, and leave it on the affected bone day and night. And then remove the bandage, and put it in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you rub the joint with gentle friction, and then reapply a soothing substance, such as a fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the use of baths, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place on that wound a warm, moist cow's hide, and press firmly upon it; and do this several times, because that is moist and will restore it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the shoulder, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
Dislocations of the hand sometimes occur, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, and the opposite is true for other joints.  However, speed is needed in the reduction at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a suppurative inflammation develops.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the hand's joint of the patient on a table, and the attendant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm over the eminence of the joint, and pushes until it returns to its place.
However, it is important to consider if the palm has already been moved to the back of the hand; in that case, the patient places the back of his hand on a table during the extension and reduction.  If the dislocation is prior to the back of the hand, then the patient's hand should be positioned on the table intrinsically, so that the physician's hand can be placed over the eminence of the joint.
And if it returns quickly, that is good.  But if not, then hold it with a bandage, avoiding inflammation, and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise neither will it endure nor will you succeed in the reduction after days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its deformity, and you find no cause for the patient's pain, but only that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are severed or crushed.  In such a case, there is no remedy but to cauterize around the injured area, and firmly apply a bandage.  This will provide some relief.
Therefore, once the injury has been reduced, apply the bandage we have described, and leave it on and secured for five days.  Then, remove the bandage and allow the patient to exercise their hand.
But if the patient's movement is restricted and you notice any heaviness, then alleviate it with warm water and gentle friction several times until relief is achieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various ways.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated towards the palm side or the back of the hand, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers, until it returns to its place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it over the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly set in its place.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising it with movements, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the back of the hand, and bind it to that part and do your first procedure on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and the vertebrae are completely displaced from their positions, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowel movements are involuntary and they cannot retain them; and often some of their limbs, or two legs or two arms, or one of them, become numb; or a vertebra is displaced, because often they are displaced and often the displacement of these occurs in four directions.
Therefore, this has already moved to the posterior parts and is called a sprain.
But the treatment for this is that you must consider if the sprain occurred due to a fall, because in that case there is no cure, nor is it completely healed.
But in those cases that arise from accidents or blows or similar events, the ancients described detailed methods of treatment, from which the most beneficial is returned; however, I have reduced this subject, and I have omitted the unnecessary detail, due to my proximity to your understanding and my exposition.  I have presented this, formed from various sources, through which I will demonstrate and expand upon it.
And of those, only the one that occurs on the back, properly speaking, is curable with what I am recommending.
And it is that the patient be laid on their face, propped up against a wall, and be supported underneath with a soft instrument so that the patient's foot is not bruised.
And then a straight pole should be placed, driven into the ground at the end of the prop near the patient's head, and another pole should be placed under the patient's feet at the other end of the prop.
And one attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in the ground; and another attendant should hold the other pole in the same way.
And then wrap around the patient's head and under the temples a strong, firm bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole positioned by the head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around the patient's hips, and around the legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole that is next to the patient's feet.
And then each attendant should extend the bandage to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be moved from their places where they are placed, but nevertheless, they should not be fixed as we have said.
And the physician places their palms on the spine with force until it returns, or places a table on it, and then they have the patient place their feet on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is roughly three arms' lengths, and hollow out a space in the wall, which we mentioned is near the patient, into which the end of the table fits. And then place half of the table on the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turning tool that is turned by hand; and that you place on the ground at the head of the patient, two poles, the length of each being one arm's length, and at the patient's feet, likewise, two poles. And the length between each pole is one palm's breadth.  A hole has been made in each pole for the turning tool to fit into, and each is firmly anchored to the ground so that it does not move.
And place a round pole on which the turning tool is placed, and turn it in the two holes of the two poles.
And at the end of that [round pole] is a hole into which the [round] pole is fitted, its length being one palm's width,  [and connected to that rotating shaft]; and in the other two poles, there is something similar to this.
And now tighten the ligatures you have tied around the patient's feet at the hole that is towards the patient's head, and the ligatures you have tied around the patient's thighs at the turning tool that is towards the patient's feet.
And now, next to each turning tool, a helper/attendant will turn the tool with their hand, while the physician adjusts the vertebra as we have described.
And once the vertebra has been repositioned and the area is adjusted, you should then apply a thick poultice with egg white, and then a bandage; then place a table-like splint (or board) on the poultice, whose width is roughly three fingers, and whose length is sufficient to cover the affected area of the vertebra.
And then it should be bound with the bandage we mentioned, and the patient should use light nourishment until he/she is healed.
And if any lingering inflammation remains at the end of healing, then medicines that soften and cleanse with table administration (as we have described) need to be administered for a long time.  And a lead plate should be applied to that area.
Sometimes inflammation occurs in the back vertebrae, caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which is now inflamed, so you should not attempt a remedy with this medicine, because it will cause your death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint can only be dislocated, and that the things that happen to other joints of slight displacement and concavity do not happen to these.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is most often dislocated inwards, and less often forwards or backwards.
A sign of a dislocation inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the groin.  The area where the groin is inflamed and painful is an abscess, because the head of the thigh bone has become stuck there.
The sign of a dislocation outwards is that the symptoms are opposite to these symptoms.
A sign of a dislocation forward is that the patient can extend their leg fully, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if they try to move it forward, they cannot;  their urine is retained, and their groin is inflamed and painful, and they walk on the toes.
A sign of a dislocation backward is that the patient cannot extend their knee, nor can they bend it forward towards the groin; the affected leg is shorter than the other, the groin is swollen, and the head of the thigh bone is towards that area.
But the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you must consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, as it has been fixed with the passage of time. If you attempt a reduction, it will remain in its position, and is not fully cured. Therefore, no presumption should be made of complete success in that case.
However, in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types, then you must try to reposition the joint, extending it forward and backward, and to the right and left, for if it easily returns, then no further treatment is needed.
But if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either with their own hands or with a bandage tied above the knee; another assistant should hold the patient's hands below their hips;  and then another assistant should apply a strong bandage around the thigh, and a third assistant should hold the end of the bandage.
And the extension should be done either from the inside towards the groin, or from the back towards the buttocks.
And perform the extension in all those ways until the patient is lifted from the ground and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type to proceed to the specific treatment.
In the proper reduction of a dislocation, when the dislocation is inward, the patient should be placed on their right side.  Then, place bandages around the root of the thigh, in the area between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.  Then, extend the bandage from above the groin to the highest point of the pubic bone.
And then another strong attendant should take the patient's arms, and firmly grasp the affected thigh and extend it outward with forceful extension, for this will return it to its proper place.
And this type is easier to treat, with this limb being more easily reduced than the other types.
But if you are excused and do not obey the entire treatment with this type of cure, then it is necessary to tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And the length of each should be about four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by about two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended over the top of a wooden post, and in a room; and their distance from the ground should be about two arm lengths.
And then order a strong young man to grasp the upper thigh, and let another young man suspend the patient, while the first young man, pressing the thigh with strength, will cause the joint to return to its place quickly.
However, in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is outward, the patient should lie on a pillow, as we have previously recounted when setting the knee; and the bandage should be tight around the affected leg and foot.
And then two poles should be placed, one at their feet, and the other at their head; and then poles fitting the middle of the hill on which damp cloths are wrapped should be placed, so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being between their thighs, so that during the extension towards the lower parts, it is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend on their respective side, and the physician with their hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be slightly extended, and it is according to this same narrative on the hill; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the patient's shin, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with this grasping, holds it extended downwards towards the front of the joint.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and they are raised from the ground; it is necessary that they be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the lifting if their hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about the patient's positioning on two surfaces; and it is on their side, and their ligaments are extended, as we have said, not much.
And it is necessary that pressure be applied with a board over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be—and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient holds their leg and extends it without excuse, then know that the limb has been returned as required—and then you join the two thighs and place a bandage and bind them so that the hip is not moved in any part; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the bandage and the splint, and compare one leg to the other.
Now, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you return and bandage that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then loosen that, and have him/her walk slowly for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, but not forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that if you ask the patient to bring their leg up to their thigh, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you should lay the patient down straight, so that if they can, they extend their leg; then place your own thigh against theirs, and place their foot between your feet; then carefully hold the knee with your palms, pressing your fingers into the knee with strength, and another assistant extends their foot so that the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that their leg adheres to the thigh with a light, effortless movement.
And then bandage that and bind the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, then remove the bandage.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strong.
But if reduction by you is unnecessary, then administer strong stretching with the bandages that were previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated by force, and the dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle often swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of the slight displacement is a gentle repositioning, and that it be extended with ease with the hands and be held until it returns to place.
But in the treatment of it when it is dislocated by force, it is necessary that you make the patient lie straight, and that one strong and capable attendant hold the affected area behind the two of them in the middle of it.
And then you, with your right hand, take hold of the foot at the highest point, and with your left hand the part of the foot just below the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot's heel with the right toward the leg without exertion.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot's heel against the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two attempts following this method, and if you see the foot is straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for that return.
But if you are unable to reduce it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and firmly place a thick cloth on the ground, and place it between the patient's thighs, and you have already draped a cloth over the patient so that it does not hurt the patient.
And then the attendant holds the patient's thighs, and another attendant extends the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the foot's ankle; and each attendant should extend opposite the other's extension, and the cloth should be stretched between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that the patient's body is not pulled down by their extension.
And then the physician straightens the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh at the lower extremities, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore, when the dislocation returns and you are sure the reduction is successful, then apply a poultice and bind it tightly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with bandages.
And you should be careful or guard against the nerves that are located behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not too tight and hurts it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage has loosened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from movement for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dislodged and corrupted, and therefore it will not receive a cure.
And if a boil/abscess arises, then you should apply to it, in accordance with what was previously mentioned in our narrative, another form of treatment and application, until it is resolved.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the fingers and toes.
The dislocated joint needs to be gently manipulated without excessive force; this is not difficult, but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then you should have the patient place their foot on a soft, even surface, like the ground or a table, and remain in that position as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on the area that seems to have the dislocation.  Then, gently press down with your foot until you see it return to its proper place, and the protrusion no longer appears.
Then place a board under the sole of the foot, extending to cover the entire foot, with two supports.  Secure it firmly and properly for three days, and then release the board and keep the patient from walking for several days, until the joint is strong and the return to normal use is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that are caused by blows, or fractures, or both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself excessively in the treatment and restoration of the patient, often death results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such a condition, but only those skilled in the art, with long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash nor presumptuous; and who, at the beginning, use only medicines that reduce feverish abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for those in whom there is hope of recovery, with alleviation of the illness, and where there appears any prospect of recovery. Then strive to perform the reduction immediately at the beginning of the condition, before it becomes a feverish abscess.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that reduces feverish abscesses and treats the wound with suitable desiccant ointments.
And if a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat their separation, and do in this regard what we have said in simple ailments regarding the treatment of similar conditions.
And, according to your ability, avoid that which is harmful in the paths of fear, just as my teaching precedes you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more beneficial to your true self in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
